والسنن ، وفي رواية أحمد وإسحاق بن راهوية ، و«مصنف ابن أبي شيبة» : عن أبي بكر الصديق قال : «أتى ماعز بن مالك النبي فاعترف وأنا عنده مرة فردّه ، ثمّ جاءَ فاعترف عنده الثانية فردّه ، ثمّ جاءَ فاعترف عنده الثالثة فردّه ، قال : فقلت له : إن اعترفت الرابعة رجمك». وقوله : كما مرّ ؛ فيسأله ما هو، وكيف هو، وأين هو، ويمن زنا، ومتى زنا. وقوله : إلا في السؤال ؛ استثناء من قوله : كما مرّ، وحاصله : إنّه قال بعض المشايخ: إنّه لا حاجة في صورة الإقرار عن سؤال متى ؛ لأنّ فائدة السؤالِ دفع ظهور التقادم، فإنّ التقادم يمنع وجوب الحد، وهذا في الشهادة، وفي صورة الإقرار على ما فإن بين حبب تلقينه برجوعه : بلعلك لمست أو قبلت ، أو وطئت بشبهة سيأتي تفصيله، والأصح أنه يسألُ في صورة الإقرار أيضاً عن متى ؛ لاحتمال كون الزنا في زمان صباه، ومثله لا يوجب الحد ، فلا بد من السؤال ليندفع هذا الاحتمال. قوله : بين ؛ أي بيّن المقر ما سئل عنه من الماهية والكيفية، والزمان والمكان، وتعين المزنية. قوله : حُبّب ؛ بصيغة المجهول من التحبيب، يعني يُستحب للحاكم أن يلقن المقر بما يرجع عن إقراره ويخاطبه بكلمات مشيرة إلى رجوعه ؛ وذلك لأنّ الحدود يحتالُ لدفعها ؛ ولذا قال النبي الله الماعز بعد إقراره مرات : «أبك جنون ، قال : لا ، قال : فهل أحصنت ؟ قال : نعم )، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم. وفي رواية لمسلم قال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد إقراره أربعاً : «فلعلك، قال: لا والله إنه قد زنا). وفي رواية للنسائي وأبي داود وأحمد قال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لماعز : «إنك قد قلتها أربع مرات فيمن ؟ قال : بفلانة ، قال : هل ضاجعتها؟ قال: نعم، قال: هل باشرتها؟ قال : نعم، قال: هل جامعتها ؟ قال : نعم). وفي رواية الحاكم في «المستدرك»، قال له رسول الله : «لعلك قبلتها ، قال : لا ، قال : لعلّك مسستها ؟ قال : لا ، قال : ففعلت بها كذا وكذا ، قال : نعم وفي «صحيح البخاري»: قال له : «لعلك قبلت أو غمزت أو نظرت؟ قال : لا ، قال : أفنكتها ؟ قال : نعم».
Know that in his statement: 'He rejects him each time' there is laxity; because it indicates that the imam rejects him four times, but this is not so. Rather, the imam rejects him three times. So when he confesses a fourth time, he does not reject him, but accepts it. Then he asks him as previously mentioned, except regarding the question of 'when'; because he only asks about it as a precaution against staleness, which prevents testimony but not confession. And it is said: he asks about 'when' as well, due to the possibility of it occurring during childhood. And so on until he confesses four times. In Sahih Muslim from Buraydah (may Allah be pleased with him): 'I was sitting with the Prophet (peace be upon him) when Ma'iz ibn Malik came and said: I have committed zina, and I want you to purify me. He said: Go back. The next day he came again and confessed zina before him. He said: Go back. Then he came a third time and confessed zina. Then he returned a fourth time and confessed. So he had a pit dug for him and placed him in it up to his chest, then ordered the people to stone him.' And his statement: 'But this is not so' indicates that confession a fourth time necessitates the hadd, as established by the narrations of the story of Ma'iz in the Sahihs and Sunan. In the narration of Ahmad, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shaybah from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Ma'iz ibn Malik came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and confessed while I was with him once, and he rejected him. Then he came and confessed a second time, and he rejected him. Then he came and confessed a third time, and he rejected him. He said: I said to him: If you confess a fourth time, he will stone you.' His statement: 'as previously mentioned' means he asks him: what it is, how it is, where it is, with whom the zina was committed, and when the zina was committed. His statement: 'except regarding the question' is an exception from his statement 'as previously mentioned'. The summary is that some scholars said: There is no need in the case of confession to ask 'when'; because the benefit of the question is to ward off the appearance of staleness, and staleness prevents the obligation of hadd. This is in testimony. In the case of confession, as for then, he should prompt him to retract: 'Perhaps you touched, or kissed, or had intercourse with ambiguity' – details to come. And the correct view is that he asks about 'when' in the case of confession as well, due to the possibility of zina occurring during his childhood, and such does not necessitate hadd. So there is a need for the question to ward off this possibility. His statement: 'clarify' means the confessor clarifies what he is asked about: the nature, the manner, the time, the place, and the identity of the woman. His statement: 'it is loved' (passive from 'tahbib') means it is recommended for the judge to prompt the confessor to retract his confession and address him with words hinting at retraction; this is because hudud are to be averted by means. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Ma'iz after his confession multiple times: 'Are you mad? He said: No. He said: Are you married? He said: Yes.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim. In a Muslim narration: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to him after his fourth confession: 'Perhaps...,' he said: 'No, by Allah, he has committed zina.' In a narration by Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, and Ahmad: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to Ma'iz: 'You have said it four times. With whom? He said: With so-and-so. He said: Did you sleep with her? He said: Yes. He said: Did you touch her? He said: Yes. He said: Did you have intercourse with her? He said: Yes.' In a narration by Hakim in al-Mustadrak: The Messenger of Allah said to him: 'Perhaps you kissed her. He said: No. He said: Perhaps you touched her? He said: No. He said: Did you do such-and-such with her? He said: Yes.' In Sahih al-Bukhari: He said to him: 'Perhaps you kissed, or touched, or looked? He said: No. He said: Did you have intercourse with her? He said: Yes.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling regarding acceptance of confession (iqrar) for zina. The author clarifies a misunderstanding: the judge does not reject the confession four times, but three times. On the fourth confession, the judge accepts it and proceeds to question the confessor about the details (nature, manner, time, place, identity of the woman). There is a difference of opinion on whether to ask about 'when' (mata) in confession: some scholars say it is unnecessary because staleness (taqadum) prevents testimony but not confession; however, the preferred view (al-ashah) is to ask, because the zina might have occurred during childhood, which would not incur the hadd penalty. The passage then discusses the recommendation (istihbab) for the judge to prompt the confessor to retract (talqin) by suggesting possibilities like a kiss, touch, or ambiguous intercourse. This is derived from the Prophet's ﷺ actions with Ma'iz, where he repeatedly asked questions that could lead to retraction. The hadith evidence from Sahih Muslim, Bukhari, and other sources is provided to support the procedure: the Prophet rejected Ma'iz three times, accepted the fourth, and then asked about sanity, marriage, and specific acts to ascertain the full truth and give opportunity for retraction.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by an adult sane person of committing zina, which triggers the hadd penalty after the fourth repetition.
تلقين (talqin): The judge's prompting of the confessor to retract his confession by suggesting alternative possibilities (e.g., kiss, touch) to avoid the hadd penalty.
تقادم (taqadum): Staleness or lapse of time since the act of zina, which prevents testimony from being accepted but does not affect confession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many times should the judge reject a confession of zina before accepting it?
A) Once
B) Twice
C) Three times
D) Four times

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi procedure for zina confession requires three rejections before accepting the fourth, followed by detailed questioning and prompting retraction (talqin), all derived from the Prophet's treatment of Ma'iz.

Judge's Encouragement for the Confessor to Retract
Commentary on the recommendation to prompt retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the judge prompting a confessor of zina to retract, identify the proof from the Sunnah, and apply the principle of dar' al-hudud.

Core Idea
The judge must encourage retraction in hudud cases; the Prophet himself did so.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the recommended practice for a judge when someone confesses to zina. The judge should suggest possibilities that could negate the hadd, such as touching or kissing, to give the confessor a chance to retract. This is based on the Prophet's treatment of Ma'iz and the principle that hudud are to be averted by doubts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
Determine the hukm level for the judge's action: is it obligatory, recommended, or permissible? The text uses 'hubbiba' which indicates istihbab (recommendation).
In Hanafi usul, 'hubbiba' signals a non-obligatory, meritorious act.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence cited: multiple hadith of the Prophet (PBUH) questioning Ma'iz and the Ghamidiyyah. The key narration is from Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, and Ahmad, where the Prophet asked about the details of the act.
The source includes several variant narrations; all demonstrate the permissibility of prompting.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling: The Prophet's questioning, such as 'Perhaps you kissed?', shows that the judge may suggest alternatives to retract. The Prophet did not rebuke Ma'iz for retracting; rather, he gave him multiple opportunities.
The Prophet's actions establish the desirability of talqin.
4
Apply the principle of dar' al-hudud
Connect to the general maxim that hudud should be warded off by doubts. The judge's prompting is a means to create a doubt that can avert the penalty.
This principle is foundational in Hanafi fiqh for all hudud cases.
5
Identify the scope and conditions
Note that the ruling applies to any confessor, but the judge should also ask about time and other details to exclude childhood or other defenses.
The commentary adds that questioning about time is necessary to avoid applying hadd for acts during non-age.

Thus, it is recommended to prompt him to retract by saying: 'Perhaps you touched, or kissed, or had intercourse under a shubha.' For he explained the prompting to retract: 'Perhaps you touched, or kissed, or had intercourse under a shubha.' Its detail will come. The correct view is that he should also be asked about the time in the case of confession, because zina may have occurred in his childhood, and such does not necessitate hadd, so questioning is necessary to negate this possibility. Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that discusses the judge's role in encouraging a confessor of zina to retract his confession. The commentator explains that it is mustahabb (recommended) for the judge to prompt the confessor by suggesting possibilities like touching, kissing, or intercourse under a shubha (doubt), thereby giving him a chance to retract. This is because the hadd penalties are to be averted as much as possible, based on the principle of warding off penalties by doubts (dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat). The proof is derived from the practice of the Prophet (PBUH) with Ma'iz and the Ghmidiyyah, where he asked suggestive questions to encourage retraction. The hadith show that the Prophet asked Ma'iz about madness, marriage, and details of the act, which implied that if he had denied any of these, the hadd would have been avoided. Therefore, the judge should similarly use gentle questioning to allow the confessor to retract. The commentary also notes that the judge should ask about the time of the zina to exclude childhood, which does not incur hadd. This is based on the correct view in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
تلقين (talqin): The act of the judge prompting or instructing the confessor to retract his confession.
حُبّب (hubbiba): Passive verb meaning 'it is recommended' (from tahbib), indicating a non-obligatory act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Retraction Before or During Hadd Execution
Ruling on the confessor's retraction before or during the execution of the hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on retraction of a confession before or during hadd execution and explain the proof from the Prophet's practice with Ma'iz and the Ghmidiyyah.

Core Idea
Retraction of a hadd confession, even mid-execution, nullifies the penalty—the confessor is released.

This lesson examines a distinctive Hanafi ruling in hudud: if a person confesses to a hadd crime (like zina) and later retracts, the punishment is dropped entirely, even if retraction occurs during the execution. The Hanafi school bases this on the Prophetic precedent with Ma'iz and the Ghmidiyyah, where the Prophet indicated that retraction should be honored. We will analyze the legal proof and the scope of retraction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is retraction (ruju') from a confession of hadd. The ruling: the confessor is released (khulliya) and the hadd is dropped, whether retraction occurs before or during execution.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is from multiple hadith regarding Ma'iz and the Ghmidiyyah, narrated by Muslim, Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, and Ahmad. These hadith explicitly state that if the confessor had withdrawn, he would not have been pursued.
The hadith are classified as ahad (zanni al-thubut) but are accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The first hadith says that if Ma'iz had withdrawn after his confession, he would not have been pursued. The second hadith extends this to the Ghmidiyyah. The third hadith shows the Prophet criticizing a companion for killing Ma'iz when he fled during stoning, implying that retraction even during execution should stop the hadd.
The Prophet's statement 'Why did you not leave him? Perhaps he would repent' directly supports dropping the hadd mid-execution.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i, Maliki) may not accept retraction after the hadd has begun, or may require additional conditions. The Hanafi school is uniquely lenient in accepting retraction at any time before the hadd is fully executed.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; both positions have textual and rational bases.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning of the hadith and the principle that the hadd is a right of Allah that can be waived by the confessor's retraction because it is based on his confession. The Prophet's explicit approval of dropping the hadd mid-execution is taken as decisive.
The mufta bihi position is that retraction is accepted at any point before the hadd is completely finished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن رجع قبل حده ، أو في وسطه خُلّي وإلا حد. [١] قوله : فإن رجع ؛ أي المقرّ عن إقراره قولاً أو فعلاً، أما قولاً فبأن يقول : كذبتُ في إقراري أو يقول : ما أقررت بالزنا ، وأما فعلاً فبهر به عند رجمه، وكذا يصح رجوعه عن الإقرار بإحصانه كذا في «البحر»)، و«الخانية». [٢]قوله : خُلّي ؛ بصيغة المجهول من التخلية أي لم يحد بالكلية إن رجع قبل حده ولم يُحَدَّ باقيه إن رجع بعد الشروع في حده، وذلك لقول بريدة له : «كنا أصحاب رسول الله لا نتحدّث أنه لو جلس ماعزّ في رحلِهِ بعد اعترافه ثلاث مرات لم يطلبه»، أخرجه مسلم. وعند أبي داود والنسائي قال : «كنا أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نتحدث أنّ الغامدية وماعز بن مالك لو رجعا بعد اعترافهما لم يطلبهما». وفي رواية لأحمد وأبي داود والنسائي : «أنه لَمّا رجم ماعز ووجد مس الحجارة خرج يشتد ، فلقيه عبد الله بن أنيس، فنزع له بوطيف بعير فقتله، وذكر ذلك للنبي ، فقال : هلا تركتموه لعله يتوب فيتوب الله عليه»
If he retracts before his hadd, or in the midst of it, he is released; otherwise, he is punished. [1] His saying: 'If he retracts' means the confessor from his confession verbally or by action. Verbally, by saying: I lied in my confession, or: I did not confess to zina. By action, by fleeing during stoning. Likewise, his retraction from the confession of ihsan (marriage) is valid, as in al-Bahr and al-Khaniyyah. [2] His saying: 'he is released' (khulliya) in the passive from takhliyah means he is not punished at all if he retracts before his hadd, and the remainder is not executed if he retracts after commencement of the hadd. This is based on the statement of Buraydah: 'We, the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, used to say that if Ma'iz had sat in his place after his confession three times, he would not have been pursued.' Reported by Muslim. And according to Abu Dawud and Nasa'i: 'We, the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, used to say that the Ghmidiyyah and Ma'iz ibn Malik, if they had retracted after their confession, they would not have been pursued.' And in a narration of Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Nasa'i: 'When Ma'iz was stoned and felt the pain of the stones, he ran away, and Abdullah ibn Unays met him, struck him with a camel's shank, and killed him. That was mentioned to the Prophet, and he said: Why did you not leave him, perhaps he would repent and Allah would accept his repentance.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling of the Hanafi school regarding the effect of retraction of a confession of zina. The matn states that if the confessor retracts before the hadd is fully executed, or during its execution, he is released and the remainder of the penalty is dropped. The commenter explains that retraction can be either verbal (e.g., saying 'I lied' or 'I did not confess') or by action (e.g., fleeing during stoning). The same applies to retraction of the confession of ihsan (marital status) – if he retracts that, the hadd is also dropped. The proof is from the practice of the Prophet (PBUH) with Ma'iz and the Ghmidiyyah, as narrated in Sahih Muslim and other hadith collections. The hadith indicate that if a confessor retracts, he should not be pursued. Furthermore, the incident where Ma'iz fled during stoning and was killed by a companion was criticized by the Prophet, who said they should have left him to perhaps repent. This supports the dropping of the hadd upon retraction. Thus, the Hanafi school accepts retraction at any time before the hadd is complete, and it nullifies the punishment.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction of the confession by the confessor, either verbally or by action.
خُلّي (khulliya): Passive meaning 'he is released'; the hadd is completely dropped or the remainder is not executed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hadd is completely stopped and the confessor is released
B) The hadd continues from the point of retraction
C) The confessor is given a lighter punishment
D) The retraction is ignored and the hadd is completed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a confessor's retraction before or during a hadd execution causes the penalty to be dropped, based on prophetical precedent emphasizing mercy and the possibility of repentance.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

Retraction of Confession Nullifies Hadd Punishment
Ruling on retraction of confession in zina before or during punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on retraction of confession before or during a hadd punishment and explain its proof from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Retraction of confession before punishment ends nullifies the hadd—a mercy rooted in prophetic practice.

In Hanafi law, a confession to a hadd crime like zina is normally binding, but the Prophet ﷺ showed that retraction is accepted even after punishment has begun. This lesson examines the ruling when the accused retracts verbally or practically before or during the execution of the hadd, and the evidence from the Sunnah that supports this leniency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the core ruling: if a person retracts confession before or during the hadd, the punishment is dropped; otherwise it is carried out.
This establishes the conditional nature of the ruling.
2
Recognize the forms of retraction
Understand that retraction can be verbal (e.g., saying 'I lied') or practical (e.g., fleeing from stoning). Both are equally valid.
Practical retraction is shown by a clear action of escaping.
3
Examine the prophetic proof
Analyze the hadith of Ma'iz and the Ghamidiyyah woman, where the Prophet ﷺ indicated that if they had retracted, they would not have been pursued. This establishes the principle that retraction stops the punishment.
The hadith is cited in multiple sources and is considered strong evidence.
4
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Practice applying the rule: if retraction occurs before the punishment starts, the entire punishment is cancelled; if during, the remainder is cancelled. The punishment already inflicted is not reverted (irrelevant here).
The timing of retraction determines whether all or part of the punishment is waived.

The Book of Hudud: Its Definition and Manner. If he retracts before his punishment or in the middle of it, he is left alone; otherwise, he is punished. [1] His saying: 'If he retracts' means the confessor retracting his confession verbally or practically. Verbally: by saying 'I lied in my confession' or 'I did not confess to zina.' Practically: by showing a strong sign at the time of stoning. Likewise, his retraction from the confession of his ihsan is valid, as in al-Bahr and al-Khaniyya. [2] His saying: 'He is left alone' is in the passive form from 'takhliyah', meaning he is not punished at all if he retracts before his punishment, and the remaining part of his punishment is not completed if he retracts after starting his punishment. This is due to the statement of Buraydah: 'We were companions of the Messenger of Allah and we used to say that if Ma'iz had sat in his place after his confession three times, he would not have been sought' — narrated by Muslim. According to Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i: 'We, the companions of the Messenger of Allah, used to say that if the Ghamidiyyah woman and Ma'iz ibn Malik had retracted after their confession, they would not have been sought.' In a narration by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i: 'When Ma'iz was stoned and felt the pain of the stones, he ran away. Abdullah ibn Unays met him and killed him with a camel's shinbone. This was mentioned to the Prophet, who said: "Why did you not leave him? Perhaps he would repent and Allah would accept his repentance." ' (1) Al-Bahr al-Raiq (5:8). (2) Sunan Abi Dawud (2:554), Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra (4:289), Musnad Ahmad (5:347), etc.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the retraction of confession in zina. It begins with the base ruling: if a person retracts their confession before or during the hadd, they are left alone; otherwise, the full punishment is carried out. The commentary explains that retraction can happen verbally (e.g., 'I lied') or practically (e.g., fleeing from stoning). Retraction regarding ihsan (restitution) is also valid. The proof comes from the hadith of Ma'iz and the Ghamidiyyah woman, where the Prophet criticized his companions for not leaving Ma'iz when he ran away, hoping he would repent. This demonstrates that retraction before punishment is effective. The sources for these narrations are cited.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujūʿ): Retraction of a confession, either verbally or practically, before or during the execution of punishment.
إقرار (iqrār): A confession made by the accused before an authority, especially of zina.

Retraction of confession before or during a hadd punishment is valid and requires the punishment to be dropped, in line with the Prophet's desire for repentance.

The Seven Conditions of Iḥṣān for Stoning Punishment
Definition of muḥṣin and conditions of iḥṣān

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven conditions of iḥṣān according to the Hanafī school and identify which condition is disputed by Imam al-Shāfiʿī.

Core Idea
Iḥṣān requires seven conditions; if any is missing, stoning does not apply.

In the Hanafī law of ḥudūd, the punishment of stoning for zinā applies only to a person who is 'muḥṣin' — one who fulfills the conditions of iḥṣān. This passage defines iḥṣān as a composite of seven specific conditions, including Islam. Understanding these conditions is crucial for distinguishing when stoning applies versus when only flogging is prescribed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of muḥṣin
The passage begins by stating that the stoning punishment is for the muḥṣin: a free, legally accountable Muslim.
2
List the seven conditions
The author enumerates: puberty, sanity, freedom, valid marriage, consummation, mutual equality in iḥṣān, and Islam.
The order is important; all must be present.
3
Note the linguistic background
The commentary explains the rare grammatical pattern of 'muḥṣin' and the various Quranic usages of iḥṣān.
This is a nuance, not a condition.
4
Recognize the dispute about Islam
Imam al-Shāfiʿī and a narration from Abū Yūsuf hold that Islam is not a condition, while the Hanafī position includes it.
This is the thamarah al-khilāf: a non-Muslim married free person would be stoned under Shāfiʿī but not under Hanafī.
5
Understand the proof for freedom
The passage cites Quran 4:25 to justify freedom as a condition, showing the legal implication that slaves are not stoned.
The verse mentions half punishment for slave women, implying stoning is for free persons only.

A section on the manner of the hadd and its establishment. It is for the muhsin: that is, a free, legally accountable Muslim. [1] His saying: 'al-muhsin' — with open or kasra of the sad. Abu al-Baqa' al-Kafawi said in his Kulliyat: 'Ihsan means chastity, and fortifying oneself from falling into the forbidden, as in His saying: "And those who accuse chaste women" (Quran 24:4). And marriage, as in His saying: "when they are married" (Quran 4:25). And freedom, as in His saying: "half of what is prescribed for free women" (Quran 4:25). And consummation, as in His saying: "you seek chastity, not fornication" (Quran 4:24). And 'muhsin' is one of those words where the active participle comes on the pattern 'muf'il' with fatha on the 'ayn, even though the standard pattern for active participle in Form IV verbs is with kasra, and the passive participle with fatha, except some exceptions, such as 'mus-hib' from 'ashaba' meaning to speak at length, and 'muflij' from 'aflaja' meaning to become bankrupt. Ihsan is the combination of seven things: puberty, sanity, freedom, valid marriage, consummation, that each spouse be like the other in the quality of ihsan, and Islam. According to al-Shafi'i, Islam is not a condition for ihsan, and similarly in a narration from Abu Yusuf, as in Kifayat al-Muntahi. [2] His saying: 'that is, a free...' The reason for requiring freedom is that the slave is not stoned; the evidence is His saying: "When they are married", meaning slave women, "then if they commit adultery, upon them is half the punishment prescribed for free women" (Quran 4:25), meaning half of what is upon the free women. The punishment upon free women and free men is of two types:...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'muhsin' and outlines the conditions for ihsan (restitution) in the context of the hadd punishment for zina. It begins by establishing that the stoning punishment applies specifically to the muhsin, defined as a free, legally accountable Muslim. The commentary then explores linguistic and legal aspects. Linguistically, 'ihsan' can mean chastity, marriage, freedom, or consummation as seen in Quranic usage. Grammatically, 'muhsin' is a rare active participle pattern from Form IV. The substantive legal definition of ihsan is a combination of seven conditions: puberty, sanity, freedom, valid marriage, consummation, mutual equality in ihsan (i.e., both spouses equally fulfil these conditions), and Islam. There is a noted difference: Imam al-Shafi'i does not require Islam as a condition, and there is a narration from Abu Yusuf holding the same view. The passage then provides a Quranic proof that freedom is required, citing the verse about slave women receiving half the punishment, implying that stoning is only for free persons. The punishment for free men and women is of two types, but the text cuts off before specifying them.

Key Terms
إحصان (iḥṣān): A legal status requiring seven conditions: puberty, sanity, freedom, valid marriage, consummation, mutual equality in ihsan between spouses, and Islam.
محصن (muḥṣin): A person who fulfills all conditions of ihsan, thus eligible for the stoning punishment for zina.

Definition of Ihsan and Proof of Stoning for the Muhsan
Defines muhsan, lists conditions, and proves stoning via abrogated verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven conditions of ihsan (muhsan) in Hanafi law, explain why Islam is a condition, and cite the proof for stoning from the abrogated verse.

Core Idea
Stoning applies only to the muhsan: a free, Muslim, married adult who has consummated — seven conditions, with Islam as the distinctive Hanafi requirement.

This passage from the commentary on Hudud defines who is subject to stoning for zina. The Hanafi school uniquely requires Islam as a condition for ihsan, a point of dispute with al-Shafi'i. We'll explore the seven conditions and the scriptural proof for stoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the term muhsan
Identify the lexical meaning of 'ihsan' from the text: it can mean chastity, marriage, freedom, or consummation. Note that the active participle 'muhsan' has an unusual vowel pattern.
The word 'ihsan' has multiple meanings; in hudud context it refers to a composite legal status.
2
List the seven conditions of ihsan
From the text, extract the seven conditions: puberty, sanity, freedom, valid marriage, consummation, equality of spouses in ihsan status, and Islam. Understand that all must be present for the stoning penalty.
The condition of Islam is distinctive to the Hanafis.
3
Analyse the dispute on Islam as a condition
Identify that al-Shafi'i and, in one narration, Abu Yusuf do not require Islam. Use the madhhab dispute map to see the thamarah al-khilaf: a non-Muslim married person would not be stoned according to Abu Hanifa.
The practical consequence is a difference in punishments for non-Muslims.
4
Locate the dalil for stoning
The text provides two proofs: the abrogated verse (الشيخ والشيخة إذا زنيا فارجموهما) and the hadith of 'Umar confirming the practice. Understand that the verse's recitation is abrogated but its ruling remains.
This is a Sunnah ahad (single narration) in terms of transmission, but the meaning is decisive.
5
Distinguish between stoning and flogging
Note that the Quranic verse (Surah al-Nur, 100 lashes) applies to non-muhsan offenders. Stoning is for the muhsan only. The abrogated verse specifies stoning for the 'old' i.e., experienced married persons.
The text mentions both punishments to show the two types of hadd.

A chapter on how to implement the hadd and its establishment. And it (the stoning) is for the muhsan: that is, for a free, legally responsible, Muslim. [1] His statement: 'for the muhsan' - with a fatha on the sad or a kasra - Abu al-Baqa' al-Kafawi said in his 'Kulliyat': 'Ihsan means chastity, and protection of oneself from falling into the unlawful, as in His statement: And those who accuse chaste women... and marriage as in His statement: when they are married... and freedom as in His statement: half of what is upon the free women of the punishment... and consummation in marriage as in His statement: seeking to be chaste, not fornicators.' Al-muhsan is one of the forms where the active participle comes on the pattern Muf'il (with a fatha on the 'ayn), although the standard for the active participle in the verb form is with a kasra, and the passive participle with a fatha, except for what has been heard. Among them: al-mushib from ashaba i.e., he talked much and at length; al-muflij from aflixa i.e., he became bankrupt. And ihsan is an expression of the combination of seven things: puberty, sanity, freedom, valid marriage, consummation, each of the two spouses being like the other in the quality of ihsan, and Islam. According to al-Shafi'i, Islam is not a condition for ihsan, and likewise according to Abu Yusuf in one narration, as in 'Kifayat al-Muntahi'. [2] His statement: 'i.e., for a free...' etc.; the reason for stipulating freedom is that there is no stoning for a non-free person. This is indicated by His statement: when they are married (i.e., slave women, meaning they are married), then if they commit an indecency, upon them is half of what is upon the free women of the punishment, i.e., half of what is upon free women. And the punishment upon free women and free men is of two types: first: lashing with one hundred stripes, which is mentioned in His statement: [Quran 24:2] 'The woman and the man who commit zina, flog each of them with one hundred stripes. And let not compassion for them overtake you in the religion of Allah, if you believe in Allah and the Last Day. And let a group of the believers witness their punishment.' Second: stoning for the muhsan, which is mentioned in His statement: 'When an old man and an old woman commit zina, stone them both absolutely, as a punishment from Allah, and Allah is Mighty, Wise.' In a narration there is an addition: 'for what they have enjoyed of pleasure' after His statement 'absolutely'. And this verse is among those whose recitation is abrogated, as reported by Abu 'Ubayd, al-Hakim, and others. And al-Bukhari and Malik in his Muwatta', and through him Muhammad in his Muwatta', reported from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab that he said in his sermon at the end of his life: 'Beware lest you perish regarding the verse of stoning, lest someone say: We do not find two hadd punishments in the Book of Allah. The Messenger of Allah stoned and we stoned. By Him in Whose hand is my soul, were it not that people would say that 'Umar has added to the Book of Allah, I would have written it: The old man and the old woman, if they commit zina, stone them both absolutely, as a punishment from Allah, and Allah is All-Knowing, All-Wise.'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on the chapter of hudud. It begins by defining the muhsan (one subject to stoning) as a free, legally responsible (mukallaf) Muslim. The author then elaborates on the term 'muhsan' citing lexical meanings from Abu al-Baqa' al-Kafawi: it can mean chastity, marriage, freedom, or consummation. Importantly, the author clarifies that in the Hanafi school, 'ihsan' for the purpose of stoning requires the combination of seven conditions: puberty, sanity, freedom, a valid marriage, consummation, both spouses being equal in the quality of ihsan and both being Muslim. The condition of Islam is a distinctive Hanafi position; the text notes that Imam al-Shafi'i and, in one narration, Abu Yusuf do not require Islam. This difference has a practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, a non-Muslim who commits zina after a valid marriage is not stoned but only flogged, while according to al-Shafi'i, stoning applies. The author then provides the proof for stoning: the Quranic verse of stoning from Surah al-Ahzab whose recitation is abrogated but whose ruling remains, and the testimony of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab confirming that the Prophet ﷺ stoned, the companions stoned, and that the ruling is established by consensus (ijma). This abrogated verse is the primary dalil for stoning the muhsan, while the verse of flogging (Surah al-Nur, 100 lashes) applies to non-muhsan (single) offenders.

Key Terms
الإحصان (al-ihsan): A composite term in the context of hudud referring to the state of being protected from hadd punishment by virtue of being a free, married, Muslim adult who has consummated the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, which of the following is a condition for being considered muhsan (subject to stoning)?
A) Marriage only
B) Marriage and freedom only
C) Marriage, freedom, and Islam for both spouses
D) Marriage and mutual consent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, stoning for zina applies only to the muhsan — a person who meets seven conditions including freedom, valid marriage, consummation, and Islam for both spouses, with the proof being the abrogated verse of stoning.

The Two Components of Hadd Zina: Lashing and Stoning
Definition of hadd for zina with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two components of the hadd for zina and cite the primary proofs from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The hadd for zina is one: lashing for non-muhsan and stoning for muhsan.

In this passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah, the commentator explains that the hadd for zina consists of two distinct penalties depending on the offender's marital status: lashing for the non-muhsan and stoning for the muhsan. The evidence for each is drawn from the Quran, the Sunnah, and the practice of the Companions. This lesson will walk you through these two components and the proofs that establish them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
The passage begins by stating that the hadd for zina comprises two things. Locate the phrases 'الجَلد مئة سَوط' and 'الرجم للمحصَن'.
This is the foundational division of the punishment.
2
Examine the Quranic proof for lashing
The author cites Surah an-Nur 24:2: 'الزَّانِيَةُ والزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا...'. The imperative verb 'fajlidu' indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
The verse is directly recited and operative.
3
Examine the proof for stoning
The author cites a verse from Surah al-Ahzab: 'إذا زني الشيخ والشيخوخة فارجموهما البتة'. This verse is abrogated in recitation (mansukh al-tilawah) but its ruling remains.
The concept of naskh al-tilawah ma‘a baqa’ al-hukm is unique to Hanafi methodology.
4
Confirm with hadith and consensus
The passage includes the hadith of ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab affirming stoning, and the hadith of the three capital crimes. It states there is ijma‘ on stoning for the muhsan.
Ijmā‘ is an independent proof that solidifies the ruling.
5
Note the implication of halving
The author argues that since stoning cannot be halved, the hadd for slaves is fifty lashes, confirming that stoning is only for free persons.
This is an inference from the principle of tanseef (halving) for slaves.

Book of Hudud: Its definition and nature.
One of them: Lashing with one hundred lashes, which is mentioned in His statement in Surah an-Nur: "The [unmarried] woman or [unmarried] man found guilty of sexual intercourse - lash each one of them with a hundred lashes, and do not be taken by pity for them in the religion of Allah, if you should believe in Allah and the Last Day. And let a group of the believers witness their punishment." (24:2)
And the second: Stoning for the muhsan, which is mentioned in His statement in Surah al-Ahzab: "When an old man and an old woman commit zina, stone them both absolutely, a punishment from Allah, and Allah is Almighty, All-Wise." In another narration there is an addition: "for the pleasure they have enjoyed" after His saying "absolutely". This verse is among those whose recitation is abrogated, as narrated by Abu 'Ubayd, al-Hakim, and others.
Al-Bukhari and Malik narrated in al-Muwatta', and through him Muhammad in his Muwatta', from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said in his sermon at the end of his life: "Beware lest you perish regarding the verse of stoning, lest someone say: We do not find two penalties in the Book of Allah. For the Messenger of Allah stoned, and we have stoned. By Him in Whose hand is my soul, were it not that people would say that 'Umar added to the Book of Allah, I would have written it: 'The old man and old woman, when they commit zina, stone them absolutely, for we have recited it.'"
And Malik also narrated from him (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: "Stoning is a right in the Book of Allah upon the one who commits zina after being muhsan, from men and women, if proof is established against him, or pregnancy, or confession."
And the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: "The blood of a Muslim is not permissible except for one of three: a man who commits zina after being muhsan, so he is stoned; a man who wages war against Allah and His Messenger, so he is killed, crucified, or exiled from the land; and a man who kills a soul, so he is killed for it." Narrated by Abu Dawud, at-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, an-Nasa'i, and others through various chains with similar wordings.
In summary: Stoning for the muhsan is established by the Quran, numerous hadiths, and the practices of the Companions; indeed, there is consensus upon it. And it is known that stoning cannot be halved, while lashing can be halved. Therefore, they agreed that the hadd of a male slave and female slave is fifty lashes. Thus, by the indication of the verse of halving, it is known that stoning is specific to free persons. As for the condition of being mukallaf—i.e., sane, not insane, adult, not a child—it is because all hudud are lifted, rather all legal obligations, from the insane and child, due to the hadith: "The pen is lifted from three: from the insane until he recovers, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the sleeping person until he wakes up." Narrated by at-Tirmidhi and others. And as for the condition of Islam, due to the hadith: "Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan." Narrated by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh in his Musnad from Ibn 'Umar marfu'an, and ad-Daraqutni narrated it in his Sunan and said: The correct opinion is that it is mauquf.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah defines the hadd (fixed punishment) for zina (unlawful sexual intercourse). The author states that the hadd consists of two components: (1) one hundred lashes for the non-muhsan (unmarried free person), citing Quran 24:2, and (2) stoning to death (rajm) for the muhsan (married or previously married free person). The evidence for stoning is a verse that was originally part of the Quran but whose recitation was abrogated, although its ruling remains. The author supports this with the hadith of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) who affirmed the practice of stoning by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Companions. The passage further establishes that stoning is established by consensus (ijma), and that it cannot be halved, unlike lashing (which can be halved for slaves). Therefore, the hadd for a slave is fifty lashes. The author then discusses the conditions for the application of the hadd: the offender must be mukallaf (adult and sane), as derived from the hadith that the pen is lifted from three types of people (the insane, the child, and the sleeping). Additionally, the offender must be a Muslim, because a non-Muslim is not considered muhsan (protected by marriage). These conditions are prerequisites for the hadd to be applied.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed punishment determined by Shari'ah for specific crimes.
المحصن (al-muhsan): A free person who has had lawful sexual intercourse in marriage (whether currently married or previously married).
الرجم (ar-rajm): The punishment of stoning to death for a muhsan who commits zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the hadd of a free muhsan who commits zina?
A) 100 lashes and exile
B) 100 lashes only
C) Stoning to death (rajm)
D) 50 lashes

Key Takeaway
The hadd for zina is one of two fixed punishments: lashing for the non-muhsan and stoning for the muhsan, both established by Quranic and prophetic proofs.

Conditions for Applying Hadd Zina: Free, Mukallaf, Muslim
Conditions for applying hadd zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for applying the hadd of zina (free, mukallaf, Muslim) and explain the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Hadd zina applies only to a free, sane adult Muslim who is either muhsan or non-muhsan.

After establishing the two components of the hadd, the passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah details the conditions that must be met for the punishment to be carried out. These conditions are derived from the Quran, Sunnah, and legal reasoning. This lesson focuses on the three conditions explicitly mentioned: the offender must be free (not a slave), mukallaf (adult and sane), and Muslim. Understanding these conditions ensures that the hadd is applied correctly and not extended beyond its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of freedom
The passage states that stoning cannot be halved, so it applies only to free persons. For slaves, the hadd is 50 lashes (half of 100). This is derived from the verse of halving (tanseef).
The condition of freedom is inferred from the inability to halve stoning.
2
Identify the condition of being mukallaf
The passage cites the hadith: 'The pen is lifted from three: the insane until he recovers, the child until he reaches puberty, and the sleeping person until he wakes up.' This shows that hadd is not applied to the insane or the child.
Mukallaf means the person is legally responsible: adult and sane.
3
Identify the condition of being Muslim
The passage cites the hadith: 'Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan.' The Hanafi position is that a non-Muslim cannot be muhsan, so the hadd of stoning does not apply; instead, the punishment is lashing or other penalties.
This hadith is narrated with a weak chain (mauquf) but accepted by Hanafis as sufficient proof.
4
Understand the practical consequences
These conditions mean that if any condition is missing (e.g., the offender is a child), the hadd is not applied. The ruler must verify each condition before executing the punishment.
These are pre-requisites, not elements of the act itself.

The author states that the hadd consists of two components: (1) one hundred lashes for the non-muhsan (unmarried free person), citing Quran 24:2, and (2) stoning to death (rajm) for the muhsan (married or previously married free person). The evidence for stoning is a verse that was originally part of the Quran but whose recitation was abrogated, although its ruling remains. The author supports this with the hadith of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) who affirmed the practice of stoning by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Companions. The passage further establishes that stoning is established by consensus (ijma), and that it cannot be halved, unlike lashing (which can be halved for slaves). Therefore, the hadd for a slave is fifty lashes. The author then discusses the conditions for the application of the hadd: the offender must be mukallaf (adult and sane), as derived from the hadith that the pen is lifted from three types of people (the insane, the child, and the sleeping). Additionally, the offender must be a Muslim, because a non-Muslim is not considered muhsan (protected by marriage). These conditions are prerequisites for the hadd to be applied.

Key Terms
التكليف (at-taklif): Legal responsibility; being an adult and sane so that one is accountable for actions.
رفع القلم (raf‘ al-qalam): The lifting of the pen of accountability for certain persons: the insane, the child, and the sleeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for applying the hadd of stoning according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) The offender must be free.
B) The offender must be married at the time of zina.
C) The offender must be Muslim.
D) The offender must be mukallaf (adult and sane).

Key Takeaway
The hadd of zina is applied only when the offender is free, mukallaf, and Muslim; these conditions are derived from clear hadith evidence and legal inference.

Conditions of Ihsan: Freedom, Adulthood, and Sanity
Defining ihsan and its undisputed conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of ihsan and identify the evidence for each condition from the text.

Core Idea
Ihsan requires freedom, sanity, adulthood, and valid marital intercourse – Islam is not required.

Stoning (rajm) is a severe hadd punishment reserved for the muhsan. But who qualifies as muhsan? This passage lays out the essential conditions: freedom, legal capacity (sanity and adulthood), and valid marital intercourse. The text also explains why stoning cannot be halved, distinguishing it from flogging for slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on stoning for the muhsan
The passage first establishes that stoning is wajib on the muhsan based on Quran, hadith, and ijma. Note that stoning cannot be halved, so it applies only to free persons.
The verse of half (Quran 4:25) indicates flogging can be halved for slaves, but stoning cannot.
2
Extract the conditions of ihsan from the text
The author lists: being free, being mukallaf (sane and adult), and having had intercourse in a valid marriage. Also, both spouses must have this status. Find the exact phrases.
The condition of valid marriage at the time of intercourse is emphasized.
3
Analyze the evidence for each condition
For freedom: the indication from the verse of half and the fact that slaves get half the lashes. For adulthood and sanity: the hadith 'the pen is lifted' from three (insane, child, sleeping).
The condition of valid marriage is from the definition; evidence is implicit in the practice of the Prophet and companions.

[۲] كتاب الحدود تعريفه وكيفيته 171 وطي بنكاح صحيح وهما بصفة الإحصان وطئ" بنكاح صحيح وهما بصفة الإحصان وأخرج الدارقطني وابن عدي عن كعب بن مالك أنه أراد أن يتزوج يهودية، فقال رسول الله : «لا تتزوجها فإنّها لا تحصنك» (١) ، وسنده ضعيف، ويعارضه ما أخرجه مالك والشيخان وأصحاب السنن وغيرهم من قصة إقامة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حد الرجمِ على اليهودي واليهودية ، فإنّه صريح في أنّ الإسلام في الإحصان ليس بشرط. وقد أجابوا عنه بأجوبة أكثرها ضعيفة، وأحسنها ما نقحه ابن الهمام من أنّ هذا الحديث دلّ على عدم اشتراط الإسلام في الإحصان، والحديث السابق القولي دل على اشتراطه، والقول مقدم على الفعل إلا أنه موقوف على ثبوتِ القول، مرفوعاً بسند صحيح، وقد بسطت الكلام في هذا المرام في التعليق الممجد علـى موطأ محمد» (٢) ، فليراجع. (1) قوله : وطئ ؛ أي بنفس الإيلاج وإن لم ينزل. كذا في «الفتح». [٢] قوله : بنكاح صحيح ؛ احتراز عن النكاح الفاسد، كالنكاح بلا شهود ؛ فإنّه لا يكون به محصناً، والشرط صحته حال الدخول، حتى لو وطئ في نكاح موقوف على الإجازة ثم أجازت العقد أو ولي الصغيرة لا يكون بهذا الوطء محصناً. [٣]قوله : وهما ؛ أي الواطئ والموطوءة، فلو زنت أمة بحر أو عبد بحـرة فـلا إحصان وكذا لو زنى بمسلمة ذمّي ثمّ أسلم لا يرجم، ثم إن بقاء النكاح ليس بشرط لبقاء الإحصان، حتى لو وطئ بنكاح صحيح في عمره مرة ، ثمَّ طلّق وزنى رجم كلّما زنى. كذا في «الدرر شرح الغرر»(4).
Stone them both, for indeed we have recited it. And Malik also narrated from him that he said: Stoning in the Book of Allah is true for the one who commits zina when he is muhsan, from men and women, if evidence is established against him, or pregnancy, or confession. And the Messenger of Allah said: The blood of a Muslim is not lawful except for one of three: a man who commits zina after ihsan, then he is stoned; a man who wages war against Allah and His Messenger, then he is killed or crucified or exiled from the land; and a man who kills a soul, then he is killed for it. Reported by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, Nasa'i, and others from different chains with similar wordings. In summary: stoning on the muhsan is established by the Quran, numerous hadiths, and the effects of the Companions; rather consensus has occurred on it. It is known that stoning cannot be halved, and only whipping is divisible; hence they agreed that the hadd of a male slave and female slave is fifty lashes. So by the indication of the verse of half, it is known that stoning is specific to free persons. As for the condition of being mukallaf, i.e., sane not insane, adult not child, it is because all hudud are lifted, rather all shar'i obligations are lifted from the insane and child due to the hadith: The pen is lifted from three: from the insane until he recovers, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the sleeping until he wakes. Reported by Tirmidhi and others. As for the condition of Islam, due to the hadith: Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan, reported by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh in his Musnad from Ibn Umar marfu'an, and by Daraqutni in his Sunan, and he said: the correct view is that it is mauquf. [2] Book of Hudud: Its Definition and Procedure. And he had intercourse in a valid marriage, both being with the quality of ihsan. And Daraqutni and Ibn Adi narrated from Ka'b ibn Malik that he wanted to marry a Jewish woman, and the Messenger of Allah said: Do not marry her, for she will not make you muhsan. Its chain is weak, and it is contradicted by what Malik, Bukhari, Muslim, the authors of the Sunan, and others reported from the story of the Prophet applying the hadd of stoning on a Jewish man and a Jewish woman. For it is explicit that Islam is not a condition in ihsan. And they responded to it with responses, most of which are weak, and the best of which is what Ibn al-Humam refined: that this hadith indicates the non-requirement of Islam in ihsan, and the previous verbal hadith indicates its requirement, and speech (qawl) is given precedence over action (fi'l), except that it is dependent on the establishment of the qawl as marfu' with a sound chain. I have expanded the discussion on this matter in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad on Muwatta' Muhammad, so refer to it. (1) His saying: had intercourse; i.e., by actual insertion even if no ejaculation. Thus in al-Fath. [2] His saying: in a valid marriage; a precaution against the invalid marriage, like marriage without witnesses; for one does not become muhsan by it. The condition is its validity at the time of entry, so even if he had intercourse in a marriage suspended on permission, and then she permitted the contract or the guardian of a minor girl, one does not become muhsan by this intercourse. [3] His saying: both; i.e., the man and the woman. So if a slave woman commits zina with a free man or a slave with a free woman, there is no ihsan. Similarly, if a dhimmi commits zina with a Muslim woman and then converts to Islam, he is not stoned. Then, the continued existence of the marriage is not a condition for the continuation of ihsan. So if he had intercourse in a valid marriage once in his lifetime, then divorced and commits zina, he is stoned every time he commits zina. Thus in Durar Sharh al-Ghurrar.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the conditions of ihsan (legal chastity that makes one liable for stoning for zina). The author first establishes the ruling of stoning for the muhsan (married person who committed zina) with evidence from the Quran, sunnah, and consensus. He then explains that stoning cannot be halved, unlike flogging, so it applies only to free persons. The conditions for ihsan are then detailed: being mukallaf (sane, adult) due to the hadith raising the pen. Then the key dispute: is Islam a condition for ihsan? A hadith says 'Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan' (reportedly marfu' but considered mauquf by Daraqutni). This is countered by the established practice of the Prophet stoning a Jewish couple, proving Islam is not required. The author notes that the qawl (saying) would take precedence over fi'l (action) but only if the qawl is authentically marfu'; since it is weak, the action is followed. The Hanafi position is that Islam is not a condition. The commentary further clarifies the definition of 'intercourse' in a valid marriage, the validity condition at entry, and that both partners must meet the same criteria. It also states that the continuation of marriage is not required for remaining muhsan.

Key Terms
الإحصان (al-ihsān): Legal chastity that makes a person liable for stoning if they commit zina; requires valid marriage, freedom, adulthood, and sanity.
المحصن (al-muhṣan): A person who meets the conditions of ihsan and is thus liable for stoning upon committing zina.
الرجم (al-rajm): The punishment of stoning to death for a muhsan who commits zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition for being considered muhsan?
A) Being Muslim
B) Being free
C) Being married at the time of zina
D) Being a descendant of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Ihsan in the Hanafi school requires freedom, adulthood, sanity, and valid marital intercourse; Islam and the continuation of marriage are not required.

Is Islam a Condition for Ihsan? The Hanafi Position
Disputing whether Islam is required for ihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the dispute on whether Islam is a condition for ihsan and explain why the Hanafi school does not require it.

Core Idea
The Prophet stoned a Jewish couple, proving Islam is not a condition for ihsan.

A key point of ikhtilaf in the law of hudud: Must a person be Muslim to be considered muhsan (liable for stoning)? Some scholars, citing a hadith, say yes. But the Hanafi school, relying on the established practice of the Prophet stoning a Jewish man and woman, holds that Islam is not required. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the scholarly method of prioritising prophetic action (fi'l) over a weak verbal tradition (qawl).

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim and its proof
Some scholars argue that Islam is a condition for ihsan. Their proof: the hadith 'Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan' and the narration from Ka'b ibn Malik that a Jewish woman cannot make a man muhsan.
These narrations are reported as marfu' (attributed to the Prophet) but are considered weak or mauquf (stopped at a companion).
2
Examine the Hanafi evidence
The Hanafi school presents the famous incident where the Prophet stoned a Jewish man and a Jewish woman for zina. This action (fi'l) explicitly shows that Islam was not a barrier to being muhsan.
This incident is reported by Malik, Bukhari, Muslim, and others, and is considered authentic.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing hadith
The author notes that the opposing hadith has a weak chain and is considered mauquf. In principle, a qawl (saying) takes precedence over fi'l (action), but only if the qawl is authentically marfu' and sound. Since it is not, the action is followed.
Ibn al-Humam's refinement is highlighted as the best response.
4
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect: if a Muslim marries a non-Muslim in a valid nikah and commits zina, the Hanafi school considers him muhsan and liable for stoning, while the opposing view does not.
This affects cases of interfaith marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author notes that the qawl (saying) would take precedence over fi'l (action) but only if the qawl is authentically marfu'; since it is weak, the action is followed. The Hanafi position is that Islam is not a condition. The commentary further clarifies the definition of 'intercourse' in a valid marriage, the validity condition at entry, and that both partners must meet the same criteria. It also states that the continuation of marriage is not required for remaining muhsan.

Key Terms
الإحصان (al-ihsān): Legal chastity that makes a person liable for stoning if they commit zina; requires valid marriage, freedom, adulthood, and sanity.
المحصن (al-muhṣan): A person who meets the conditions of ihsan and is thus liable for stoning upon committing zina.
الرجم (al-rajm): The punishment of stoning to death for a muhsan who commits zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not require Islam for ihsan?
A) Because the Quran does not mention Islam as a condition.
B) Because the Prophet stoned a Jewish couple, showing Islam is not required.
C) Because the hadith requiring Islam is abrogated.
D) Because the Sahaba unanimously agreed on this.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that Islam is not a condition for ihsan, relying on the authentic prophetic action of stoning a Jewish couple over a weak verbal hadith.

Conditions of Ihsan and Stoning Procedure for the Muhsan
Commentary on conditions of ihsan and stoning procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of ihsan according to the Hanafi school, apply the rule that Islam is not a condition, and describe the stoning procedure including the role of witnesses.

Core Idea
A married adulterer is stoned to death; the Prophet stoned Jews, so Islam is not required for ihsan.

The hadd penalty for zina by a muhsan (married person) is stoning. But who qualifies as muhsan? And how exactly is the stoning carried out? This passage from 'Umdat ar-Ri'aya explains both, focusing on a key dispute: must the muhsan be Muslim? The Hanafi school holds that valid marriage and freedom suffice, drawing on the Prophet's stoning of a Jewish couple. The passage also details the procedure: an open space, death by stoning, and witnesses throwing the first stones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of ihsan
List the elements that make a person muhsan: valid marriage, freedom, and whether Islam is required. The passage mentions the hadith of the Jewish couple to argue Islam is not required.
Pay attention to the distinction between conditions at the time of intercourse and the continuation of ihsan.
2
Analyze the dalil for the Hanafi position
The Prophet’s action of stoning a Jewish man and woman is the proof. Identify its type (Sunnah fi'liyyah), its transmission (mutawatir in meaning), and its implication (no Islam requirement).
Also note the weak verbal hadith that suggests the opposite, and how Ibn al-Humam reconciles them.
3
Trace the dispute and tarjih
The Shafi'i school requires Islam. See the opposing dalil (the weak hadith) and the Hanafi response: the action is stronger and the verbal hadith is not proven as marfu'.
The practical thamarah al-khilaf: a non-Muslim muhsan is stoned in Hanafi law.
4
Understand the stoning procedure
Stoning is in an open space (fida'), continues until death, and the witnesses begin the stoning. The reason for witnesses starting is to confirm their testimony and allow retraction.
Derivative rulings: if a mahram stones him, no disinheritance; if anyone kills the condemned after judgment, no qisas or diyah, but ta'zir for acting without authority.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case, determine if the person is muhsan and thus liable to stoning. Consider marriage validity, freedom, and whether the ruling changes if the marriage ended.
Remember: once muhsan, always muhsan – divorce does not remove ihsan.

Book of Hudud: Its definition and manner. Intercourse in a valid marriage while both are in the state of ihsan. Ad-Daraqutni and Ibn Adi narrated from Ka'b ibn Malik that he wanted to marry a Jewish woman, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not marry her, for she will not make you muhsan.' Its chain is weak, and it is contradicted by what Malik, al-Bukhari, Muslim, and the authors of the Sunan and others narrated from the story of the Prophet (peace be upon him) implementing the punishment of stoning on a Jewish man and woman, for this is explicit that Islam is not a condition in ihsan. They answered with answers, most of which are weak, and the best of them is what Ibn al-Humam refined: that this hadith indicates that Islam is not a condition for ihsan, while the previous verbal hadith indicates its condition, and a saying takes precedence over an action, but this is conditional upon the saying being established as marfu' with a sound chain. I have expanded the discussion on this in 'at-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad', so let it be consulted. (1) His saying: 'intercourse' means by mere insertion even if he did not ejaculate. As in 'al-Fath'. [2] His saying: 'in a valid marriage' excludes an invalid marriage, like marriage without witnesses, for he is not made muhsan by it. The condition is its validity at the time of entry, so if he has intercourse in a marriage that is suspended upon permission and then she permits the contract or the guardian of a minor permits, he is not muhsan by this intercourse. [3] His saying: 'both' meaning the one who has intercourse and the one with whom he has intercourse. So if a slave woman commits adultery with a free man, or a slave with a free woman, there is no ihsan. Similarly, if a dhimmī commits adultery with a Muslim woman and then converts to Islam, he is not stoned. Then, the continuation of the marriage is not a condition for the continuation of ihsan. So if he had intercourse in a valid marriage once in his life, then divorced and committed adultery, he is stoned whenever he commits adultery. As in 'Durar Sharh al-Ghurar'. (4) 162 'Umdat ar-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya: 'He is stoned in an open space until he dies; his witnesses begin stoning him.' That is, intercourse while both are in the state of ihsan, meaning the matters by which ihsan is established—excluding intercourse—were present before this intercourse. So when intercourse occurs, all that establishes ihsan is completed. So his saying: 'It is for the muhsan' is a beginning, and its predicate is his saying: 'He is stoned in an open space until he dies; his witnesses begin stoning him.' [2] His saying: 'beginning' contains a leniency that is not hidden, for the beginning is only the pronoun, and his saying 'for the muhsan' is among its dependents, but such leniency does not harm the intended purpose, and its like has passed in the chapter on tayammum in the 'Book of Purity'. [3] His saying: 'in an open space'—with kasrah on the fa'—is the desert and a spacious place. Its origin is the stoning of Ma'iz in the prayer area as reported in the two Sahihs and others. The prayer area at that time was a spacious open area. The reason is that a spacious place is more suitable for stoning him and more distant from the possibility that some stoners might hit others. [4] His saying: 'until he dies' indicates that the purpose is to kill him by way of stoning, not just stoning in general, as attested by the authentic hadiths in the Sahihs. Derived from this is what is mentioned in 'al-Bahr' and 'al-Muhit' and others: if his mahram stones him, he is not disinherited. Yes, he should not intend to kill him because there are enough others. Also derived from this: if a person kills the adulterer who is to be stoned, or gouges out his eye after the judgment of stoning, his blood is wasted; no retaliation on the killer if he killed intentionally, and no blood money if unintentional, but he should be disciplined for acting without the imam's authority, as in 'an-Nahr'. [0] His saying: 'begin with him' meaning the witnesses of his adultery stone him first, even with a small pebble, because they might be bold in giving testimony, then find the direct act great and retract. Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on stoning (rajm) for the muhsan (married person who commits adultery). It opens by clarifying the conditions of ihsan: valid marriage (excluding invalid contracts like without witnesses), both parties must be free and Muslim? (a dispute is presented), and the marriage must be valid at the time of intercourse. The text then discusses the famous hadith about a Jewish man and woman being stoned by the Prophet (ﷺ), which indicates that Islam is not a condition for ihsan, contrary to some hadith. The best resolution, per Ibn al-Humam, is that verbal hadith takes precedence over action, but requires sound chain. The commentary then explains that the continuation of marriage is not required for the continuation of ihsan; once muhsan, always muhsan. The second part details the procedure: stoning is in a spacious open place (like desert), continues until death (not just symbolic), and the witnesses start the stoning (even with small stones) to confirm their testimony and to possibly avert the punishment if they retract. Derivative rulings: if a mahram stones him, he is not disinherited but should not intend to kill; if anyone kills the condemned adulterer after judgment, it is without retaliation or blood money, but he is disciplined for taking the law into his own hands.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): The state of being muhsan, meaning having had valid sexual intercourse in a valid marriage while being free (and in some views Muslim). It makes one liable to stoning for zina.
مُحصن (muhsan): A person who has attained ihsan, i.e., a free adult who has consummated a valid marriage. Such a person is punished by stoning for zina.
رجم (rajm): Stoning to death as a hadd punishment for zina committed by a muhsan or by a non-muhsan on certain conditions.
فضاء (fida'): An open, spacious area (desert, plain) where stoning is carried out.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first action in the stoning procedure?
A) The imam throws the first stone.
B) The witnesses of the adultery throw the first stone(s).
C) The community gathers and throws stones simultaneously.
D) The condemned person chooses his own executioner.

Key Takeaway
A muhsan is stoned to death for zina; the Hanafi school does not require Islam for ihsan, and the procedure mandates an open space, death by stoning, and witnesses throwing the first stones.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

Witnesses Must Begin Stoning: The Hanafi Procedure for Rajm
Commentary on the stoning procedure and the witnesses' obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of witness-initiated stoning in the Hanafi school and explain the hikmah behind it as a stratagem to avert the hadd.

Core Idea
Witnesses must begin stoning to reveal any doubt; their recantation averts the hadd.

In the previous discussion, the conditions of ihsan were clarified. Now the procedure of executing the stoning punishment unfolds with a unique Hanafi rule: the witnesses who testified against the muhsan must personally cast the first stones. This lesson examines the legal basis, underlying wisdom, and scholarly responses to an objection drawn from the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The act is stoning the muhsan. The condition is that witnesses begin. If they do not, the hadd falls.
2
Locate the dalil
The practice of Ali (radiallahu anhu) as narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. Also the rational of testing witnesses.
Dalil is a qawl sahabi (statement of a Companion).
3
Follow the objection from the Ma'iz case
Some argue the imam's presence is not necessary because the Prophet did not attend Ma'iz's stoning. Ibn al-Humam responds.
Response: Ma'iz confessed, so no need to test witnesses.
4
Determine the practical consequence
If witnesses refuse/die/are absent, hadd falls. If someone kills the adulterer after sentence, no qisas/diyah but ta'zir.
Refusal is not explicit recantation, so no qadhf punishment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'i.e. the things...etc.'; he indicated by this explanation to repel what is imagined that ihsan is conditional upon intercourse in a valid marriage, so it occurs after the intercourse, so how can they be considered muhsan at the time of intercourse, as is implied by the author's wording that the state and the one in that state are concurrent in time. [2] His statement: 'a subject' (mubtada'); there is an inaccuracy in it that is not hidden, for indeed the subject is only the pronoun, and his statement 'for the muhsan' is among its dependents, but such an inaccuracy does not harm the original purpose, and its equivalent has passed in the chapter on tayammum from the Book of Purification. [3] His statement: 'in an open space' (fi fada') - with kasra of the fa': it is the desert and a vast place, and its origin is the stoning of Ma'iz in the musalla, as narrated in the Sahihain and others, and the musalla at that time was a vast open space, and the secret in it: that the vast space is more possible for his stoning and farther from the possibility that some of the stoners may hit each other. [4] His statement: 'until he dies'; he indicated thereby that the intent is to kill him by way of stoning, not merely stoning, as is attested by the authentic hadiths narrated in the Sahih collections. And from it branches what is mentioned in al-Bahr and al-Muhit and others: that if his relative stones him, he is not prevented from inheritance; yes, he should not intend to kill him, because others suffice. And from it also branches: that if a person kills the adulterer who is required to be stoned or gouges out his eye after the decree of stoning, his blood is waste; there is no retaliation against the killer if his killing was intentional, and no blood money if it was accidental, and he should be disciplined (ta'zir) for acting without the imam, as in al-Nahr. [0] His statement: 'begin with him' (yabda'u bihi); i.e. the witnesses of his zina stone him first, even if with a small pebble; because they may bold themselves to bear testimony, then they consider the direct action terrible and recant, so there is a stratagem to avert the hadd, and it is sought, as in al-Muhit. And its origin is what was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah in his Musannaf: 'Indeed, Ali radiya Allahu anhu, when the witnesses testified before him about zina, ordered the witnesses to stone, then the people stoned him, and if it was by confession, he began and stoned, then the people stoned.' (1) And this beginning is obligatory, as in al-Dhakhira, and mentioned in others as a condition, and likewise the imam's stoning in the case of witness testimony after the witnesses' stoning, and in the case of confession initially. So if the imam refrains from stoning, it is not permissible for the people to stone him because they know that the condition of stoning has been lost. And an objection is raised against it: that it contradicts the stoning of Ma'iz radiya Allahu anha in the time of the Prophet, where the Prophet sallallahu alayhi wa sallam did not attend and did not stone him himself, as narrated in the previous reports. Hence Ibn Kamal al-Rumi in Idah al-Islah stated: 'The presence of the imam is not necessary,' and it was approved in al-Nahr al-Fa'iq. (2) And Ibn al-Humam responded in al-Fath (3): that the reality of what is indicated by the saying of Ali is that it is obligatory upon the imam to order the witnesses to begin as a stratagem to establish the indication of recantation or its absence, and that he begins in the case of confession to show the people his lack of leniency in some conditions of judgment and hadd; so if he refrains, an indication of recantation appears and the hadd is prevented due to the appearance of doubt, and this is absent in his case (the Prophet's), so his not stoning is not evidence for the fall of the hadd. [1] His statement: 'falls' (saqata); i.e. the hadd of stoning; because the condition is the beginning of the witnesses, given that in the case of their absence they might have recanted their testimony, and in the case of their death the testimony is void, and the condition is its continuation up to the time of stoning, and in the case of their refusal to stone the matter is more obvious, but they are not punished with the hadd of qadhf due to refusal, because it is not explicit in recantation, so it suffices for the fall of the hadd of the adulterer but does not suffice for establishing the hadd of qadhf against them. As in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the procedure of stoning a married adulterer (muhsan). It begins by addressing a potential confusion: how can a person be considered muhsan at the time of intercourse if ihsan requires a valid marriage and intercourse that occurs after? The commentator explains that all conditions of ihsan except the act itself exist prior to the intercourse, and the intercourse completes the condition, so they are muhsan at the moment of the act. The text then explains the terms used in the original ruling: 'in an open space' (fada') means a desert or spacious area, exemplified by the stoning of Ma'iz in the musalla, which was open. The wisdom is to avoid hitting each other. 'Until he dies' indicates that the purpose is death by stoning, not just stoning. From this, several rulings branch: if a relative stoned him, the relative still inherits; if someone kills the adulterer after the sentence, there is no qisas or diyah but ta'zir for bypassing the imam. The main focus is on 'begin with him' meaning the witnesses start the stoning, even with a small pebble, as a stratagem (hila) to see if they recant, thereby averting the hadd. The origin is from Ali's practice. This beginning is obligatory. If witnesses refuse, are absent, or die, the hadd falls. An objection based on the case of Ma'iz where the Prophet did not attend is answered by Ibn al-Humam: the Prophet's absence does not negate the obligation on the imam to order the witnesses, but rather shows that the condition of recantation was not applicable in his case. The commentator also clarifies that witnesses who refuse to stone are not punished for qadhf because refusal is not explicit recantation.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): Legal status of being a free, adult, married Muslim who has had valid intercourse; makes zina punishable by stoning.
رجم (rajm): Stoning to death as the hadd punishment for a muhsan who commits zina.
شهود (shuhud): Witnesses of the zina; they must start the stoning in the Hanafi procedure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the witnesses start the stoning in the Hanafi school?
A) To honor the witnesses
B) As a stratagem to allow them to recant, averting the hadd
C) Because the imam is not allowed to stone
D) To intimidate the adulterer

Key Takeaway
The witnesses must initiate the stoning as a wajib condition; their refusal or absence causes the hadd to fall.
