Initiating Stoning in Hadd for Zina: The Imam's Role
Ruling on who begins stoning and conditions for fall of hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who initiates stoning in hadd for zina and identify the conditions under which the hadd falls.

Core Idea
Stoning requires the Imam's initiation; if witnesses refuse or die, the hadd falls.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we examine the precise procedure for implementing the hadd of stoning (rajm) for zina. The central question is: who must throw the first stone? The answer differs depending on whether the case is proven by testimony or confession. This ruling is not merely procedural; it safeguards against executing the hadd when doubts arise, such as possible retraction of testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the hukm level of the Imam's initiation of stoning. The text states it is wajib (obligatory) and a condition for the hadd to be valid.
The imperative form in the hadith indicates obligation per Hanafi usul.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib in Ibn Abi Shaybah's Musannaf, and the Sunan Abi Dawud hadith on the Ghamidiyyah woman.
Both are ahad hadith, so the proof is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling. The phrase 'أمر الشهود أن يرجموا' shows the Imam's command; the imperative indicates obligation.
The Hanafi principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the objection raised by Ibn Kamal al-Rumi: the Prophet did not attend Ma'iz's stoning, so presence is not necessary.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the Sahibayn are not mentioned here.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand Ibn al-Humam's response: the condition is ordering, not physical presence. The Prophet's absence does not negate the condition because he may have delegated.
The mufta bihi position requires the Imam's initiation; if absent or refraining, the hadd does not proceed.

فإن أبوا، أو غابوا ، أو ماتوا سقط ، ثم الإمام
الحد، وهو مطلوب ، كذا في «المحيط».
وأصله ما أخرجه ابن أبي شَيْبَةَ في «مصنِّفه»: «إنّ عليا لله كان إذا شهد عنده الشهود على الزنا، أمر الشهود أن يرجموا ثم يرجمه الناس، وإذا كان بإقرار بدأ هو فرجم ثم رجم الناس» (١). وهذه البداية واجبة، كما في «الذخيرة»، وذكرها في غيرها بلفظ الشرط، وكذا
رجم الإمام في صورة الشهادة بعد رجم الشهود، وفي صورة الإقرار ابتداء، فلو امتنع الإمام عن الرجم لا يحل للقوم رجمه لعلمهم بفوات شرط الرجم.
ويرد عليه : إنه مخالف لرجم ماعز رضي الله عنها في زمان النبي ، حيث لم يحضره النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولم يرجمه بنفسه على ما شهدت به الروايات السابقة، ومن ثم ذكر ابن كمال الرومي في «إيضاح «الإصلاح»: إنّ حضور الإمام ليس بلازم، وأقره في «النهر الفائق» (٢).

(1) قوله : سقط ؛ أي حد الرجم ؛ لأنّ الشرط هو بداية الشهود، على أن في صورة غيبوبتهم يحتمل رجوعهم عن الشهادة، وفي صورة موتهم انتفت الشهادة ، والشرط بقاؤها إلى وقتِ الرجم ، وفي صورة إبائهم عن الرجم الأمر أظهر، لكن لا يحدون حد القذف بالإباء ؛ لأنه ليس صريحاً في الرجوع، فيكفي في سقوط حدّ الزاني ، ولا يكفي في إقامة حد القذف عليهم. كذا في «الفتح».
ثُمَّ النَّاسِ)
وفي المقر يبدأ الإمام ، ثم الناس

من المؤمنين ،
وأخرج عن مجاهد الله قال : الطائفة الثلاثة فصاعداً، وأخرج عبد بن حميد وابن المنذر وابن أبي حاتم عن قتادة الله قال : أمر الله أن يشهد عذابهما طائفة . قال : ليس ذلك للتفضيح ، وإنما ذلك ليدعوا الله لهما بالتوبة والرحمة. (٢) قوله : يبدأ الإمام ؛ لما ثبت في سنن أبي داود وغيره أنه: «رجم رسول الله المرأة الغامدية بحصاة، وكانت أقرّت بالزنا : ثم قال للناس: ارموا واتقوا الوجه» (٣). وأخرج البيهقي عن علي الله أنه قال : «أيها الناس، أيما امرأة جيء بها، وبها حبل أو اعترفت، فالإمامُ أوّل مَن يرجم، ثمّ الناس، ثمّ رجمها، ثم أمرهم، فرجم صف، ثمّ صف، ثم صف» (٣).
يرمي
وفي رواية لأحمد عنه الله أنه قال : «لو كان شهد على هذه أحد لكان أوّل مَن الشاهد يشهد، ثم يتبع شهادته حجره ، ولكنها أقرّت، فأنا أوّل من رماها ،
فرماها بحجر، ثم رماها (الناس)
Book of Hudud / Definition and Procedure: If they refuse, or are absent, or die, it falls. Then the Imam (must act). The hadd is sought. Likewise in al-Muhit. Its origin is what Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated in his Musannaf: 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) when witnesses testified before him about zina, he ordered the witnesses to stone (first), then the people stoned. And if it was by confession, he began and stoned then the people stoned.' This beginning is obligatory, as in al-Dhakhirah, and mentioned elsewhere as a condition. Likewise, the Imam's stoning in the case of testimony is after the stoning of the witnesses, and in the case of confession initially. If the Imam refrains from stoning, it is not permissible for the people to stone him because they know the condition for stoning is missing. It is objected that this contradicts the stoning of Ma'iz (may Allah be pleased with him) in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him), where the Prophet did not attend and did not stone him himself, as the previous narrations attest. Hence Ibn Kamal al-Rumi in Idah al-Islah stated that the presence of the Imam is not necessary, and it was confirmed in al-Nahr al-Faiq. Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath responded that the reality of what 'Ali's statement indicates is that it is obligatory for the Imam to order the witnesses to begin as a precaution; to establish the indication of retraction or its absence, and he begins in the case of confession; so that it becomes clear to the people that he is not lenient in some conditions of judgment and hadd. So if he refrains, the sign of retraction appears, and the hadd is prevented due to the emergence of doubt. This is absent in his case, so his not stoning is not evidence for the fall of hadd. Footnote: 'Saqata' means the hadd of stoning falls because the condition is the beginning of the witnesses; in case of their absence, it is possible they retracted their testimony; in case of their death, testimony is void, and the condition is its continuation until the time of stoning; in case of their refusal to stone, the matter is more obvious, but they are not punished for false accusation (qadhf) due to refusal because it is not explicit retraction; it suffices for the fall of the zani's hadd, but does not suffice for establishing the hadd of qadhf against them. Likewise in al-Fath. Then the people. And in the case of the confessor, the Imam begins, then the people. Likewise the hadd falls from the zani if some of his witnesses leave the eligibility for testimony due to fisq, blindness, muteness, or qadhf. Likewise in Minah al-Ghaffar. 'Then the people' indicates that stoning should be in the presence of people; hence it is recommended in an open space. And the verse supports it: 'And let a group of the believers witness their punishment' (Quran 24:2) – meaning the male and female fornicator. Al-Hasan said: the group is ten. Narrated by Abd ibn Hamid. Al-Zuhri said: the group is three or more. Narrated by Ibn Jarir. And from Mujahid: the group is three or more. And Abd ibn Hamid, Ibn al-Mundhir, and Ibn Abi Hatim narrated from Qatadah: Allah commanded that a group witness their punishment. He said: that is not for humiliation, but so that they may pray for their repentance and mercy. Footnote: 'The Imam begins' because it is established in Sunan Abi Dawud and others that the Messenger of Allah stoned the Ghamidiyyah woman with a pebble, and she had confessed to zina, then he said to the people: 'Stone and avoid the face.' And al-Bayhaqi narrated from 'Ali that he said: 'O people, whichever woman is brought with pregnancy or she confesses, the Imam is the first to stone, then the people.' Then he stoned her, then ordered them, so they stoned row by row. And in a narration by Ahmad from him: 'If someone had testified against her, the first to throw would be the witness, then his stone would follow his testimony. But she confessed, so I am the first to throw at her.' So he threw a stone at her, then the people threw.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the procedure for implementing stoning (rajm) in hadd for zina. The text first states a general condition: if the witnesses refuse to stone, are absent, or die, the hadd falls. Then it addresses the role of the Imam. The core ruling is that in cases of testimony, the witnesses must begin stoning; in cases of confession, the Imam begins. This is supported by a narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib. The obligation of the Imam to order the witnesses or to begin himself is emphasized. The text then addresses an objection: the story of Ma'iz where the Prophet did not personally stone. The response clarifies that the condition is about the Imam ordering the witnesses as a precaution to check for retraction. For confession, the Imam begins to show seriousness. The condition for stoning is the initiation by witnesses, and if they refuse, the hadd falls; but they are not punished for qadhf because refusal is not explicit retraction. Additionally, if witnesses later lose eligibility (fisq, blindness, muteness, or being convicted of qadhf), the hadd also falls. The passage also recommends stoning in the presence of a group of believers, citing Quran 24:2, and various interpretations of 'group' (ten, three, etc.), with the wisdom being to pray for the offenders' repentance, not humiliation. Finally, additional hadith evidence from Abu Dawud and al-Bayhaqi confirms that the Imam begins with confession, and witnesses begin with testimony. This elaboration by the commentator expands upon the base text of al-Wiqayah.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): The prescribed punishment for a crime, here specifically stoning for zina.
الإمام (al-Imam): The ruler or his deputy who oversees the implementation of hadd punishments.
الشهود (al-shuhud): The witnesses who testified to the act of zina.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Retraction of testimony; if witnesses retract, the hadd drops.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school conditions the implementation of rajm on the Imam's initiation, and if witnesses refuse or become ineligible, the hadd falls.

Order of Stoning: The Imam Begins
Commentary on the order of stoning and who begins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who initiates stoning, distinguish between confession and testimony cases, and cite the primary proofs.

Core Idea
The imam stones first; testimonial cases may differ, but the established practice follows the Prophet.

This lesson examines a commentary on the order of stoning (rajm) for the hadd of zina. The matn simply says 'then the people,' but the commentator expands on who begins and why. We will explore the Quranic call for a group to witness, the hadith evidence from the Ghamidiyyah woman and Ali, and the crucial distinction between cases of confession and testimony. Additionally, we touch on a condition that can cause the hadd to be dropped entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn and its expansion
Locate the base text 'ثم الناس' and see how the commentator (Imdad al-Ri'aya) clarifies that the imam begins in the fixed place, then the people.
The comment 'وفي المقر يبدأ الإمام' modifies the matn.
2
Understand the Quranic witness requirement
Examine verse 24:2 commanding a 'ta'ifah' of believers to witness the punishment. The commentator gives various interpretations of 'ta'ifah' (three, ten, etc.) and its purpose (not disgrace, but supplication).
This establishes the recommended public nature of stoning.
3
Analyze the hadith proofs for the imam beginning
Study the hadith of the Ghamidiyyah woman: the Prophet stoned first then instructed the people. Also, Ali's practice of stoning first after confession. These form the primary evidence.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud, Musnad Ahmad, and al-Bayhaqi.
4
Distinguish between confession and testimony
The commentary includes a narration from Ali differentiating: if witnesses testify, they begin; if confession, the imam begins. Note how this nuance affects the ruling.
Some Hanafi authorities hold that the imam always begins, but this passage preserves both views.
5
Note the additional ruling on hadd dropping
If before execution some witnesses lose eligibility (due to fasiq, blindness, muteness, or qadhf), the hadd is dropped. This is a separate condition affecting the punishment's validity.
Referenced from Manh al-Ghaffar; it cautions against procedural flaws.

ولغير المحصن جلده مئة وسطاً بسوط لا ثمرة له).

صفاً خلف صف، لئلا يصيب بعضهم بعضاً» (٢).
Then the people. And in the fixed place, the imam begins, then the people. Then the people. And in the fixed place, the imam begins, then the people. Likewise, the hadd (punishment) falls from the adulterer if some of his witnesses leave the eligibility of testimony due to immorality, blindness, muteness, or slander (as in Manh al-Ghaffar). 1. His saying: 'Then the people' – he indicated by it that stoning should be in the presence of people; therefore, it is recommended that it be in an open space. And the evidence for him is His saying: 'And let a group of the believers witness their punishment' (Quran 24:2), i.e., the adulterer and adulteress. Al-Hasan said: the group is ten – reported by Abd ibn Hamid. Al-Zuhri said: the group is three or more – reported by Ibn Jarir. And from Mujahid: the group is three or more. And Abd ibn Hamid, Ibn al-Mundhir, and Ibn Abi Hatim reported from Qatada: Allah commanded that a group witness their punishment. He said: that is not for disgrace, but rather so that they supplicate to Allah for them for repentance and mercy. 2. His saying: 'The imam begins' – due to what is established in Sunan Abi Dawud and others that: 'The Messenger of Allah stoned the Ghamidiyyah woman with a pebble, and she had confessed to adultery, then he said to the people: throw and avoid the face.' And al-Bayhaqi reported from Ali that he said: 'O people, any woman brought forth, and she is pregnant or confesses, then the imam is the first to stone, then the people, then he stoned her, then he ordered them, so they stoned row by row by row.' And in a narration by Ahmad from him: 'If someone had testified against her, the first would be the witness who testifies, then his testimony would be followed by his stone, but she confessed, so I am the first to throw at her, and he threw a stone at her, then the people threw.' And for the non-muhsan, one hundred lashes with a medium whip without a thong. In al-Mughrib: al-thamara is the 'adhabah, which is its tail. And in the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba from him: 'Zina is of two types: secret zina and public zina. Secret zina is when witnesses testify, so the witnesses are the first to throw, then the imam, then the people. Public zina is when pregnancy or confession appears, so the imam is the first to throw.' And from him in Sunan al-Bayhaqi: 'He ordered the stoners to line up like the prayer rows, row behind row, so that some of them would not hit others.'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, specifically on the rulings of the hadd of zina. The author, 'Imdad al-Ri'aya, explains two main points: the order of stoning and an additional ruling on the dropping of hadd. 

First, the matn states 'Then the people,' meaning after the imam, the people participate in stoning. The commentator clarifies that in a fixed place (al-miqar), the imam begins, then the people. He also notes that if some witnesses lose their eligibility (due to fasiq, blindness, muteness, or having been slandered), the hadd punishment for zina is dropped. This is from 'Manh al-Ghaffar'.

The commentary then delves into the meaning of 'the people' in the Quranic verse (24:2) 'and let a group of believers witness their punishment.' Various early authorities define 'ta'ifah' (group) as three, ten, or more. The wisdom is not disgrace but supplication for their repentance.

On the imam beginning, the commentator provides hadith evidence: the Prophet stoned the Ghamidiyyah woman first, then told the people to throw, avoiding the face. Ali ibn Abi Talib also practiced this order. A narration from Ahmad distinguishes between cases where witnesses are present (they start) and cases where the person confesses (the imam starts). The passage also mentions that the stoners should line up in rows like prayer rows to avoid injury.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shariah for certain crimes like zina.
الرجم (al-rajm): Stoning to death as the hadd for a married (muhsan) adulterer.
الطائفة (al-ta'ifah): A group of believers witnessing the punishment; interpreted as at least three by majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who begins the stoning when a person confesses to zina?
A) The witnesses
B) The imam
C) The people
D) The confessor himself

Key Takeaway
The imam initiates stoning as a sunnah; the distinction between confession and testimony affects who begins, but the relied-upon Hanafi ruling is that the imam starts.

Washing, Shrouding, and Praying Over the Stoned Person
Ruling with proof on funeral rites for stoned persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing, shrouding, and praying over a person executed by stoning, and cite the primary evidence from the actions of Ali and the Prophet.

Core Idea
A person executed for hudud is still a Muslim and merits full burial rites.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a question that may arise: does Islamic law treat the body of a major sinner with dignity in death? The commentator explains that the stoned person is to be washed, shrouded, and prayed over like any other Muslim. The ruling is grounded in the instruction of Ali ibn Abi Talib and the confirmed practice of the Prophet Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The passage states that the stoned person is washed, shrouded, and prayed over. These are obligations (fard kifayah) for the living towards the dead.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The commentator cites the command of Ali: 'Do to her what you do to your dead.' This equates the stoned person with any other deceased Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Ali's instruction directly links the treatment of the stoned person to the general treatment of the dead. The word 'what you do to your dead' encompasses ghusl, kafn, and salat.
4
Examine supporting evidence
The Prophet's prayer over the Ghamidiyyah woman (Sahih Muslim) and over Ma'iz (Sahih al-Bukhari) provides direct prophetic precedent.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Thus, the stoned person receives full rites. There is no distinction based on the sin or punishment; Islamic identity is preserved.

The basis of it is the saying of Ali after he stoned a woman: 'Do to her what you do to your dead' – al-Bayhaqi narrated it in his Sunan. And it has been reported in Sahih Muslim and the four Sunan that the Prophet prayed over the Juhaniyyah (Ghamidiyyah) woman who had confessed to zina. And in Sahih al-Bukhari: that he prayed over Ma'iz.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'He is washed, shrouded, and prayed over.' The commentator explains that the stoned person (whether muhsan or non-muhsan) is to be treated like any other Muslim who died: given a ritual bath (ghusl), wrapped in a shroud (kafn), and the funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) is performed. The primary evidence is the action of Ali ibn Abi Talib, who after stoning a woman, instructed the companions to treat her as they treat their dead. This is supported by authentic hadiths: The Prophet prayed over the Ghamidiyyah woman (who was stoned) and also over Ma'iz. This establishes the ruling that the hadd punishment does not deprive the deceased of the rights of a Muslim.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The ritual washing of the deceased body, including the stoned person, as per normal Islamic burial.
التكفين (al-takfin): Shrouding the deceased in white cloth, here applied to the stoned person.

The Description of the Whip for Flogging a Non-Muhsan
Ruling on the type of whip used for the hundred lashes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required characteristics of the whip for hadd flogging according to the Hanafi school and identify the proof from the practice of the Companions.

Core Idea
The whip for hadd must be painful but not wounding – medium between severity and softness.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the definition of a 'medium whip' for flogging a non-muhsan. It explains that the whip should cause pain but not cut the skin, and it must be adjusted if the offender is weak. The proof is drawn from the practice during Umar's caliphate, where the whip's cutting tip (thamar) was removed and the whip softened.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states 'medium' – the Hanafi ruling is that the whip must be between one that cuts and one that is painless. This is a required condition for the hadd punishment.
2
Understand the Definition
The commentator defines 'medium' as 'painful but not wounding' (mu'lim ghayr jarih). This distinguishes it from a whip that lacerates or one that is too soft to cause pain.
The term 'thamar' refers to the tip or thong that can cut.
3
Note Individual Adjustment
If the offender is of weak build and flogging might cause death, the flogging must be lightened to what he can bear. This shows the Hanafi principle of tailoring punishment to the individual's capacity.
This adjustment is based on the same prophetic spirit of avoiding excessive harm.
4
Examine the Proof
The commentator cites the practice during Umar's time: the whip's thong was cut off, then it was pounded between two stones to soften it. This is narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba. A similar hadith from Abd al-Razzaq shows the Prophet initially called for a severe whip, but the context is incomplete; the point is moderation.
The proof is an action of the Companions (amal al-sahabi), which serves as evidence for the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
12 ] قوله : وسطاً ؛ أي سوطاً متوسطاً بين الجارح وبين غير المؤلم، بأن يكون مؤلماً غير جارح، ولو كان المجلودُ ضعيف الخلقةِ فخيف هلاكه يجلد جلداً ضعيفاً يحتمله. كذا في «الفتح»(4). والأصل في هذا الباب ما أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة في مصنفه» عن أنس له أنه قال : «كان يؤمر في زمان عمر بالسوط فتقطع ثمرته ، ثم يدق بين حجرين حتى يلين، ثمّ يضرب به». وأخرج عبد الرزاق في مصنفه»: «إن رجلا أتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : يا رسول الله : إني أصبتُ حداً فأقمه علي، فدعا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بسوط شديد له ثمرة ، فقال
12. His saying: 'medium' – i.e., a whip that is between one that cuts and one that is not painful, so that it is painful but not wounding. And if the one being flogged is of weak build and it is feared he might die, he is flogged with a light flogging that he can bear. Similarly in al-Fath. The basis in this chapter is what Ibn Abi Shayba narrated in his Musannaf from Anas that he said: 'In the time of Umar, the whip used to be ordered, so its thong was cut off, then it was pounded between two stones until it softened, then they would beat with it.' And Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf: 'A man came to the Prophet and said: O Messenger of Allah, I have committed a hadd, so establish it upon me. So the Messenger of Allah called for a severe whip with a thong, then he said...'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn statement 'medium' regarding the whip for flogging a non-muhsan. The commentator defines 'medium' as a whip that is between a lacerating one and a painless one—it should cause pain but not cut the skin. Moreover, if the offender is physically weak, the flogging should be lightened to what he can bear. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle of tailoring punishment to the individual's capacity. The textual basis is the practice during Umar's caliphate: the whip was prepared by removing its thong (the tip that could cut) and softening it between stones. A hadith shows the Prophet initially called for a severe whip with a thong, but the context (possibly the man's strength) is not fully recorded here; the point is that the whip should be moderate as per the sunnah practice.

Key Terms
الثمرة (al-thamarah): The tip or thong of a whip that can cut; it is removed to avoid injury beyond the intended punishment.
السوط (al-sawt): The whip used for flogging in hadd punishments; must be medium and without harmful projections.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that the whip for flogging a non-muhsan be medium – painful but not cutting – and adjusted for the weak, based on the practice of the Companions.

The Requirement for a Smooth Whip in the Hadd Punishment
Commentary on the requirement that the whip have no knots or sharp tips.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the whip in the hadd lashing of a non-muhsin and identify the dalil from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
The whip for hadd must be medium and free of any thamara — no knots or sharp tips.

This lesson examines a seemingly minor but legally significant detail: the physical state of the whip used in the hadd punishment for zina. The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of the Companions and explicit Prophetic guidance, requires the whip to be free of any protrusions (thamara) that could cut the skin. This reflects the broader principle that hudud, while punitive, must not be excessive or injurious.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states 'بسوط لا ثمرة له' — the whip must have no thamara. Determine what thamara means and why it is prohibited.
The commentator discusses two meanings: knot (uqdah) and tail (adhabah).
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are cited: (1) The practice of Umar (رضي الله عنه) where the thamara was cut and the whip softened. (2) The hadith of a man who came to the Prophet (ﷺ) and the Prophet rejected a whip with thamara and selected a medium one.
The hadith is from Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's rejection of a whip with thamara and his selection of a medium whip indicates that the whip must be free of any sharp or injurious features. The imperative 'أمر' (he ordered) implies obligation in this context.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The commentator resolves the lexical ambiguity of thamara: it can mean a knot or the tail-tip. The preferred view is that both must be avoided to prevent injury.
Al-Mughrib and al-Fath support the inclusive interpretation.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the whip must be medium and free of any hard protrusion. If a whip with thamara is used, the punishment is still valid but the act is makruh tahrimi.
This is the position of the Hanafi school, as stated by the commentator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Hudud / Its Definition and Manner. And he is washed, shrouded, and prayed over. For the non-muhsin, his lashing is one hundred (lashes) with a medium whip having no thamara. In al-Mughrib: al-thamara is al-'adhabah, which is its tail. And it is narrated in the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba from him (Allah be pleased with him): 'Zina is of two types: secret zina and public zina. Secret zina is when witnesses testify, so the witnesses are the first to throw, then the Imam, then the people. Public zina is when pregnancy or confession appears, so the Imam is the first to throw.' And it is narrated from him in Sunan al-Bayhaqi: 'He ordered the stoners to line up like the rows of prayer, row behind row, so that some do not hit others.' He said: 'And he is washed,' i.e., the stoned person is washed like other deceased, and shrouded like their shrouding, and prayer is performed over his funeral. The basis for this is the saying of Ali (Allah be pleased with him) after stoning a woman: 'Do with her as you do with your dead,' recorded by al-Bayhaqi in his Sunan. And it is recorded in Sahih Muslim and the four Sunans: 'The Prophet (Allah bless him and give him peace) prayed over the Juhayni woman al-Ghamidiyyah who confessed to zina,' and in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'He prayed over Ma'iz.' [2] He said: 'medium,' i.e., a whip that is moderate between one that cuts and one that does not harm, such that it is painful but not injurious. If the person being flogged is weak in constitution and death is feared, he is flogged with a light flogging that he can bear. This is in al-Fath. The basis for this chapter is what Ibn Abi Shayba recorded in his Musannaf from Anas (Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'In the time of Umar, they used to take a whip and cut its thamara, then pound it between two stones until it softened, then beat with it.' And Abd al-Razzaq recorded in his Musannaf: 'A man came to the Prophet (Allah bless him and give him peace) and said: O Messenger of Allah, I have committed an offense requiring a hadd, so apply it to me. The Messenger of Allah (Allah bless him and give him peace) called for a strong whip having a thamara, then said: A whip less than this. Then he was brought a whip and said: A whip more than this. Then he was brought a whip between the two whips, and he said: This, and ordered it and he was flogged.' [1] He said: 'And it is said: al-'uqdah (the knot),' with dammah, in Persian: kareh. This is the well-known interpretation of thamara, but al-Mughrib preferred that thamara is the tail of the whip, which is with two fatha, in Persian: rishta-yi taziyaneh, and similarly al-'adhabah. And it is mentioned in al-Fath: 'It is narrated from Anas (Allah be pleased with him) that he used to order that the whip's thamara be cut, then it is pounded between two stones until it softens, then he strikes with it. The intent is that he should not strike with it while its tip is hard because it would cut. So how about if it has a knot? The conclusion is that one avoids both the thamara in the sense of a knot and in the sense of the branch that makes two tails, to include the shared word in negation. And if one used thamara metaphorically for what resembles a knot to include via metaphor what has a dry tip as we mentioned, it would be better, because it should not be struck with such a whip until its head is pounded and it becomes medium.' End of summary.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Hidayah regarding the whip used for the hadd punishment of lashing for the non-muhsan (unmarried fornicator). The matn states 'with a medium whip having no thamara.' The commentator first clarifies the ruling that the condemned person is washed, shrouded, and prayed over, citing the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) praying over those stoned, and the action of Caliph Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). He then explains 'medium' means a whip that inflicts pain but does not cut, referencing the practice of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) where the whip's tip was removed. The author then provides hadith evidence from the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba and Abd al-Razzaq, showing that the Prophet (peace be upon him) rejected a whip with a thamara and selected a medium whip. The passage then delves into the lexical meaning of 'thamara', presenting two interpretations: (1) it is the knot at the end (al-'uqdah), and (2) it is the tail or tip (al-'adhabah, al-dhanab). The commentator, following al-Mughrib and al-Fath, indicates that both must be avoided to ensure the whip does not cause injury. Ultimately, the relied-upon ruling is that the whip must be free of any hard or sharp protrusion, and if its tip is hard, it must be softened before use.

Key Terms
الثمرة (al-thamara): The tip or knot at the end of a whip; the part that could cause injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must the whip satisfy for the hadd lashing of a non-muhsan?
A) It must be thick and heavy.
B) It must be medium and without thamara.
C) It must have a knot to cause more pain.
D) It must be a sword.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the whip for hadd to be medium and free of any thamara (knot or tip) to prevent injury, based on Prophetic practice.

Procedure of Hadd Lashing: Covering Awrah and Distribution of Lashes
Explanation of the rulings on exposure and distribution of lashes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings on exposure and distribution in the hadd lashing and explain the rationale (talil) behind each.

Core Idea
The body must be covered and lashes spread—preserving life and dignity even in punishment.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary explains how the hadd lashing for the non-muhsan is carried out. It addresses two key details: which clothing remains and where the lashes may strike. The commentary gives the reasons (talil) rooted in avoiding harm and protecting the condemned's dignity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the matn
Read the matn: clothes are removed except the izaar, and lashes are distributed over the body except head, face, and private parts. Note these two distinct rulings.
The izaar is a waist-wrapper covering from navel to knee.
2
Locate the reasons provided by the commentator
The commentary explains: 'because removing it would expose the private part, which is forbidden' for the izaar, and 'because concentrating them in one place may lead to death' for the distribution.
These are ta'lil (causation) based on the principles of avoiding haram and preventing destruction of life.
3
Understand the exceptions and their reasoning
The head, face, and private parts are excepted from lashing. The commentary states it is 'for this very reason'—striking these areas is particularly dangerous.
The exceptions follow from the same principle of avoiding death or severe harm.
4
Note any ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is clear: the izaar must remain and lashes must be distributed. Other schools may differ on the extent of clothing removal or areas struck, but the passage does not elaborate.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih, but the details are not provided here.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the procedure for carrying out the hadd lashing for the non-muhsan. The matn states that the condemned person's clothes are removed except the izaar (waist-wrapper), and the lashes are distributed over the body except the head, face, and private parts. The commentator provides two reasons: First, the izaar is kept on to cover the awrah (private parts), as removing it would be haram (forbidden). Second, the lashes are spread out to avoid concentrating them in one area, which could cause death. The exceptions (head, face, private parts) are precisely because striking these areas is particularly dangerous or inappropriate.

Key Terms
الإزار (al-izaar): A waist-wrapper or loincloth covering the lower body from navel to knee.
التفريق (al-tafriq): Distribution; here, the act of spreading lashes over different parts of the body.
العورة (al-'awrah): Private parts that must be covered in the presence of others.

Method and Conditions of Flogging in Hudud
Commentary on the method and conditions of flogging in hudud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method of flogging (jald) in hudud, identify the conditions for its validity (such as removal of clothes except izār, distribution of lashes, avoidance of face/head/private parts), and explain the proofs from sunnah and ijmāʿ.

Core Idea
Flogging in hudud must avoid the face and private parts, distribute lashes to prevent death, and use a whip with the knot removed—mercy even in punishment.

Flogging (jald) is a prescribed punishment for certain hudud offences such as zina and qadhf. The Hanafi school, following the practice of the Prophet (ṣallallahu ʿalayhi wa sallam) and the rightly-guided caliphs, has detailed the method and conditions to ensure the punishment achieves its deterrent and reformative purpose without causing excessive harm or violating human dignity. This lesson unpacks the commentary on al-Wiqāyah, explaining the rules for the implement, the body, and the posture of flogging.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The method of flogging in hudud. The commentary prescribes a set of actions (removing clothes except izār, distributing lashes, avoiding face/head/private parts, removing the thamara, standing posture, no madd). Some are wajib (avoiding face, distribution), some sunnah (standing, sitting for women), and some prohibitions (madd, using whip with knot).
The ruling level is composite: wajib for conditions preventing harm, sunnah for the overall method.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the hadith 'If any of you flogs, let him avoid the face' (Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim) as the primary proof for avoiding the face. It also references athar from 'Ali (ra) about standing/sitting, and the practice of 'Umar (ra) for slaves. The commentary also relies on the principle of preventing death (distribution) and preserving 'awrah (izār).
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning (tawatur ma'nawi) as it is reported by multiple companions.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'فليتق الوجه' (let him avoid the face) uses the imperative verb 'فليتق', which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation (wujub). Thus, avoiding the face is wajib, not just recommended. Similarly, the prohibition of uncovering the 'awrah (izār removal) is based on the general prohibition of exposing the 'awrah.
The distribution of lashes is derived from the goal (maqsad) of preventing death, not from a direct text, so it is based on ijtihad.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., the Shafi'i school) allow flogging only on the back, arguing that concentration on the back is less harmful. The Hanafi school insists on distribution over the body (excluding head, face, private parts) to avoid death. This disagreement is based on different interpretations of the hadith and the objective of the punishment.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and supported by the practice of the early caliphs.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi tarjih relies on the hadith's command to avoid the face, the universal principle of avoiding harm (darar), and the practice of 'Ali and 'Umar. The commentary prefers the interpretation that the thamara (knot) must be removed to prevent injury. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and al-Tahawi support these rulings, making them part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāyah explains the proper method of flogging (jald) in hudud punishments. The commentary opens with the ruling that the offender's clothes are removed except the izār (loincloth) because uncovering the 'awrah is haram. The lashes are to be distributed over the body, not concentrated in one area, to avoid causing death; for the same reason, the face, head, and private parts are excluded. The term 'thamara' is discussed: it refers to the knot at the end of the whip (or the tail), and it must be avoided to prevent injury; if the whip is dry, its end should be softened between stones. Then the commentary addresses the position of the offender: flogging is done standing (for men) and sitting (for women). The whip is not to be stretched or raised high above the head, as this causes extra pain. The slave receives half the punishment (50 lashes instead of 100) based on Q. 4:25 and the practice of 'Umar. A master cannot flog his slave without the imam's permission. The commentary cites several hadith and athar to support these rulings.

Key Terms
ثمرة (thamara): The knot at the end of a whip, or its tip/tail. The text discusses two meanings: 'uqda (knot) and dhanab (tail).
إزار (izār): A waist-wrapper covering from navel to knees; the minimum covering for 'awrah.
مد (madd): Stretching the whip or the person; prohibited because it increases pain beyond the intended punishment.

Master's Authority to Execute Hadd on Slaves: The Hanafi Response to Shafi'i
Hanafi response to Shafi'i on master's authority over hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a master's authority to execute hadd on a slave and explain the key distinction between hadd and ta'zir that underlies the ruling.

Core Idea
Hadd is a right of Allah, so only the ruler may execute it; ta'zir is discretionary, so the master may do it.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute concerning the authority of a master to impose fixed punishments (hudud) on his own slave. While the Shafi'i school grants the master full authority to execute hadd based on his general authority over the slave, the Hanafi school limits this to discretionary punishment (ta'zir), reserving hadd exclusively for the ruler. Understanding the basis of this difference reveals the Hanafi principle that hadd punishments are rights of Allah and require representation of the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold? State that only the ruler (Imam) or his deputy may execute hadd punishments. The master has no such authority.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school objects? Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the master has complete authority (wilayah) over his slave and may execute hadd just as he may execute ta'zir.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does al-Shafi'i cite? The master's general authority and reports from some Companions who flogged their slaves, as recorded in al-Muwatta.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut this? By distinguishing hadd from ta'zir: hadd is a right of Allah, does not fall by waiver, and must be executed by a deputy of the Shari'ah (the ruler). Ta'zir is a right of the slave (or over the slave) and may be delegated.
The Hanafi also cite sayings of Hasan al-Basri and 'Ata' al-Khurasani that hadd is for the ruler.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? Under Shafi'i, a master may flog his slave for zina himself. Under Hanafi, the master must refer the case to the ruler.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا عندنا خلافاً للشَّافِعِي شهوتهن، وهذا هو النكتة في تقديم ذكرِ الزانية على الزاني في قوله : ( الزانية والراني فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِائَةَ جَلْدَةٍ ) وقدم ذكر الرجل في آية السرقة، وهي قوله : ( وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَمُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا ) ؛ لأن مبنى السرقة على الاختفاء، واتخاذ الحيل، ومثله يحصل في الرجال أكثر بالنسبة إلى النساء. على أن شاهد الوجودِ أيضاً يشهد بكثرة السرقة في الرجال، وكثرة الزنى في النساء، فقدّم في كلّ من الآيتين ما هو أكثر وقوعاً ؛ ولهذه النكتة قدم ذكر الرجل في آية الرجم ؛ لأن الزنى بعد الإحصان في الرجل أكثر بالنسبة إلى النساء. قوله : خلافاً للشافعي ، له أن للسيد ولاية كاملة على عبده وأمته، فتجوز له إقامة الحدودِ كما يجوز له إقامة التعزير، ومثله روي عن بعض الصحابة له أنه جلد عبده، كما في «الموطأ». ولنا : إنّ الحد حق الله ، ولذا لا يسقط بإسقاط العبد، فيستوفيه من هو نائب عن الشرع، وهو الإمام أو نائبه، بخلاف التعزير، فإنّه حق العبد. كذا في «الهداية» ، ويشهد لمذهبنا قولُ الحسن البصري : «أربع إلى السلطان: الصلاة والزكاة والحدود والقصاص)، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وعن عطاء الخراساني ه قال : «إلى السلطان : الزكاة والجمعة والحدود».
This, according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (Explanation of the wisdom in mentioning the adulteress before the adulterer in the verse, and the thief before the thief? Actually: 'And this is the subtle point in giving precedence to mentioning the adulteress over the adulterer in His saying: 'The adulteress and the adulterer, flog each one of them a hundred lashes.' And He gave precedence to mentioning the man in the verse of theft, which is His saying: 'And the thief, male and female, cut off their hands' because the basis of theft is concealment and resorting to tricks, and such occurs in men more than in women. Moreover, the witness of existence also testifies to the abundance of theft in men and the abundance of adultery in women, so in each of the two verses He gave precedence to what occurs more. And for this subtle point, He gave precedence to mentioning the man in the verse of stoning, because adultery after ihsan occurs in men more than in women.) (Commentary on the phrase 'contrary to al-Shafi'i': He (al-Shafi'i) holds that the master has complete authority over his male and female slave, so it is permissible for him to execute hadd punishments just as it is permissible for him to execute ta'zir. And similar to this is reported from some Companions that he flogged his slave, as in al-Muwatta. And our evidence: Had is a right of Allah, and therefore it is not dropped by the slave's dropping (of the claim), so it is carried out by one who is a deputy of the Shari'ah, which is the Imam or his deputy, unlike ta'zir, for it is a right of the slave. Thus in al-Hidayah. And the saying of al-Hasan al-Basri testifies for our madhhab: 'Four things are for the ruler: prayer, zakat, hadd punishments, and retaliation.' Narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba. And from 'Ata' al-Khurasani: 'For the ruler: zakat, Friday, and hadd punishments.')
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the authority of a master to execute hadd punishments on his own slave. The author begins by explaining the wisdom behind the order of mention in the Quranic verses: in the verse on adultery (24:2), the adulteress is mentioned before the adulterer because adultery occurs more frequently in women, while in the verse on theft (5:38), the thief (male) is mentioned first because theft is more common among men. This same reasoning applies to the verse on stoning (implied), where the man is mentioned first since post-marital adultery is more common among men. Then the author presents the Shafi'i position: the master has full authority over his slave, so he may execute hadd punishments just as he may execute ta'zir (discretionary punishment). This is supported by reports from some Companions who flogged their slaves. The Hanafi response (from al-Hidayah) is that hadd is a right of Allah and does not fall by the slave's waiver; therefore, it must be carried out by the ruler or his deputy, who represents the Shari'ah. Ta'zir, on the other hand, is a right of the slave (or the master?) and can be executed by the master. The author then cites Hasan al-Basri and 'Ata' al-Khurasani to support the Hanafi view that hadd punishments are exclusively for the ruler (sultan).

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for certain crimes (e.g., zina, theft, intoxication, qadhf). It is considered a right of Allah.
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment determined by the judge or authority for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who must execute hadd punishments?
A) The master of the slave
B) The Imam or his deputy
C) Any Muslim adult
D) The victim of the crime

Key Takeaway
The key distinction between hadd and ta'zir determines authority: hadd is a right of Allah executed by the ruler; ta'zir is discretionary and can be executed by the master.

Procedure for Flogging a Woman and Stoning: Clothing, Sitting, Digging, and No Combination
Rulings on stripping a woman's clothes, digging a pit, and not combining flogging with stoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the three Hanafi procedural rulings for executing hadd punishments on women and men, and identify the hadith proofs that support them.

Core Idea
A woman's clothing is not fully stripped; only pain-blocking garments are removed; stoning alone suffices for muhsan, no flogging added.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains three key procedural aspects of executing hadd punishments under Hanafi law: how a woman is to be flogged, whether a pit is dug during stoning, and whether flogging and stoning are combined. The rulings balance the purpose of punishment (deterrence and pain) with modesty and the established Sunnah. Let us examine each ruling with its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
List the three distinct rulings presented: (1) during flogging of a woman, only the fur and padded garments are removed; (2) she is flogged while sitting; (3) digging a pit is permissible for a woman but not for a man; (4) flogging and stoning are not combined.
The passage integrates two separate topics: flogging of women and stoning (which includes digging and combination).
2
Examine the rationale for each ruling
For (1): Completely stripping would expose awrah; removal of fur and hashw ensures pain reaches the body while modesty is preserved. For (2): Sitting provides stability and modesty. For (3): Digging for women provides extra covering; men do not need it. For (4): The consistent prophetic practice shows stoning alone.
The rationale is grounded in the objectives of the punishment: deterrence without indecency.
3
Analyze the dalil from the Sunnah
Identify the specific hadith: stoning of al-Ghamidiyyah (digging for women), the practice of Ma'iz (no digging for men), and the absence of flogging in any of the stoning reports. Note the conflicting report from Buraydah about digging for Ma'iz.
The author prioritizes the practice of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and the majority over Buraydah's solitary report.
4
Note the operative rulings for fatwa
Based on the proofs, the mufta bihi positions: removal of fur/hashw is wajib; sitting is required; digging for women is mustahabb, not wajib; no pit for men; no combination of flogging and stoning.
5
Consider the broader principle
These rulings reflect the Hanafi commitment to avoiding unnecessary harshness and preserving dignity, even in punishment.

And her clothes are not removed except the fur and padding, and she is flogged while sitting. And digging (a pit) is permissible for her, not for him. And there is no combination of flogging and stoning. (Commentary:) The reason for this: stripping her would expose her private parts. Al-Fur (with fath of the fā' and sukūn of the rā') is in Persian: yūstīn. And al-Hashw (same meter) is in Persian: jāma-yi nambadār. Since both prevent the pain from reaching the body of the one being flogged, and covering is achieved without them, their removal is necessary. (His statement: 'And digging is permissible for her' indicates by mentioning permissibility that it is not obligatory, for the purpose of digging is to achieve covering, which is already achieved by her clothes. But since it provides extra covering, digging for her up to her chest is recommended. This is established in Sahih Muslim concerning the stoning of al-Ghamidiyyah in the prophetic time, and similar is reported from 'Ali by Ahmad and al-Bayhaqi. (His statement: 'Not for him') means that it is not dug for the man, because there is no need for it. This is supported by the saying of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'When the Prophet ordered the stoning of Ma'iz, we took him out to al-Baqi'. By Allah, we did not tie him nor did we dig (a pit), but he stood for the people, and we threw at him with bones, clods of earth, and pottery. He ran and we ran after him until he reached 'Ard al-Harrah, and we threw at him with the large stones of the Harrah until he became silent.' Narrated by Muslim. And contradicting it is what Muslim also narrated from Buraydah: that he dug a pit for him up to his chest. (His statement: 'And no combination of flogging and stoning') means that flogging and stoning are not combined for the adulterer who is muhsan, but rather stoning alone suffices, as the reports indicate. For it is established in the Sahih and Sunan collections the story of the stoning of Ma'iz, the story of the stoning of al-Ghamidiyyah, and the story of the stoning of a woman who committed adultery with her husband's hired man, and flogging is not reported in any of them.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings related to the execution of hadd punishment for adultery (zina). First, when flogging a woman, her clothes are not completely removed; only the fur (yūstīn, a fur garment) and the padding (jāma-yi nambadār, a padded garment) are taken off, because the purpose of flogging is pain, and these garments would prevent it. Completely stripping her would unveil her awrah, which is prohibited. The woman is flogged while sitting, not standing. Second, it is permissible (but not obligatory) to dig a pit for the woman to stand in during stoning, up to her chest, to provide extra covering and modesty. This is recommended (mustahabb) based on the practice of stoning al-Ghamidiyyah. However, for a man, no pit is dug, as there is no need for it; the stoning of Ma'iz was done without a pit, and that is the preferred practice, though there is a conflicting report from Buraydah about digging for Ma'iz. The author notes this difference but gives weight to the majority practice. Third, for the muhsan adulterer (married or previously married), stoning alone is prescribed, and flogging is not combined with stoning. This is proven by the consistent practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the cases of Ma'iz, al-Ghamidiyyah, and the woman whose husband was hired, where only stoning was carried out without additional flogging.

Key Terms
فرو (fur): A fur garment that covers the body and would prevent the pain of flogging from reaching the skin.
حشو (hashw): A padded garment (like a quilted jacket) that similarly prevents pain.
رجم (rajm): Stoning to death as the hadd punishment for a muhsan adulterer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Standing
B) Lying down
C) Sitting
D) Kneeling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances punishment with modesty and follows the prophetic practice: for women, only pain-blocking garments are removed, she sits, digging a pit is recommended for stoning, and stoning alone suffices for muhsan without flogging.

Ruling on Removing Clothes for Flogging a Woman
Clarifies what clothes are removed during flogging for a woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which garments must be removed during flogging for a woman according to the Hanafi school, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Only fur and padding are removed to avoid uncovering awrah while ensuring pain reaches the body.

This passage addresses a specific detail within the hudud punishments: the procedure for flogging a woman. The Hanafi school balances the need for effective punishment with the obligation to preserve the woman's modesty. The ruling identifies that only two outer garments—the fur coat and the padded lining—must be removed, while all other clothes remain. The rationale is that these garments block pain, and keeping them would defeat the purpose of the punishment, while removing all clothes would expose her awrah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is flogging a woman for hudud; the ruling is that only two specific garments are removed: the fur (al-faru) and the padding (al-hashw).
2
Locate the proof
The proof is a rational argument (dalil aqli): stripping all clothes uncovers awrah (which is prohibited), while leaving all clothes blocks pain. Thus a middle ground is taken.
The text says: 'الوجه فيه : إنّ في تجريدها كشف العورة' (the reason is that stripping her would uncover her awrah).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The reason (wajh) is that the fur and padding are specifically the garments that prevent pain from reaching the body. Since modesty (satr) is maintained without them, their removal is necessary (lazima).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may require full stripping for maximum deterrence, but the Hanafi school prioritizes modesty as an overriding principle.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school, as it balances the two competing legal interests: effective punishment and preservation of awrah.
Authority weight: zahir riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُنْزَعُ ثيابها إلا الفرو والحشو [1]قوله : ولا ينزع ...الخ ؛ الوجه فيه : إنّ في تجريدها كشف العورة. والفرو : - وهو بفتح الفاء وسكون الراء المهملة – بالفارسية : يوستين. والحشو: على وزنه بالفارسية : جامه ينبه دار، لما كانا مانعين عن وصول الألم إلى جسد المضروب والستر حاصل بدونهما لزم نزعهما.
And her clothes are not removed except the fur and padding. [1] He said: 'And her clothes are not removed...' etc. The reason is that stripping her would uncover her awrah. The fur - it is with fatha on the fa and sukun on the ra - in Persian: yusteen. And the padding: on its weight in Persian: jama yanbah dar. Because these two prevent the pain from reaching the body of the one being flogged, and modesty is achieved without them, so their removal is necessary.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion on the procedure for flogging a woman guilty of hudud. The ruling states that during flogging, the woman's clothes are not removed except for the outer fur garment and the inner padding. The commentator explains the reasoning: removing additional clothes would uncover her private parts (awrah). The terms 'fur' and 'padding' are defined in Persian: fur is a type of coat, and padding is a lined garment. Since these two items block the pain from reaching the body and modesty is maintained without them, they must be removed. This ruling emphasizes preserving modesty while carrying out the punishment.

Key Terms
الفرو (al-faru): A Persian garment (yusteen) worn over clothes; specifically the outer coat.
الحشو (al-hashw): A padded garment (jama yanbah dar) that cushions the body; also removed to allow pain to reach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school mandates that during flogging of a woman, only garments that block pain (fur and padding) are removed, while all other clothes remain to preserve her awrah.

Position of Woman During Flogging: Sitting
Ruling on the position of a woman during flogging

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a woman is flogged sitting for hudud offenses and explain the underlying rationale of modesty and safety.

Core Idea
A woman is flogged sitting to preserve her dignity and reduce harm.

This brief ruling from the matn establishes a key gender-based distinction in the execution of hudud punishments. While men are flogged standing, women are flogged sitting. The Hanafi school codifies this to safeguard modesty and prevent unnecessary injury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on the position of a woman during flogging (jald) for a hadd offense. The hukm is that she must be sitting. The Arabic 'wa tuhaddu jalisah' (وتحد جالسة) directly states this.
The verb 'tuhaddu' is passive feminine, indicating the ruling is specific to women.
2
Contrast with the male position
Though not stated here, the general Hanafi ruling is that men are flogged standing. This distinction is based on differences in physique and modesty requirements.
The rationale is that sitting prevents the woman's garments from falling or shifting, maintaining her cover ('awrah).
3
Understand the wisdom (hikmah)
The sitting position reduces the force of the blows and protects the woman’s dignity. It also prevents injuries to the back and internal organs that might occur if she were standing.
This reflects the principle of raf' al-haraj (removing difficulty) within the bounds of the punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحد جالسة.
And she is flogged while sitting.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This is a direct ruling from the matn that a woman undergoing flogging for a hudud offense should be in a sitting position. The commentator does not provide a separate explanation, but the ruling is clear. The wisdom likely includes maintaining her dignity and preventing injury.

Key Terms
وتحد جالسة (wa tuhaddu jalisah): The phrase from the matn meaning 'and she is flogged while sitting'—the core ruling that a woman's flogging position is sitting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Standing
B) Sitting
C) Lying down
D) Kneeling

Key Takeaway
A woman must be flogged sitting to preserve her modesty and prevent injury.

The Ruling on Digging a Pit for a Woman during Flogging
Clarifies the permissibility and recommendation of digging a pit for women during hudud punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on digging a pit for a woman during flogging and explain why it is recommended and not obligatory, citing the evidence.

Core Idea
Digging a pit for a woman during flogging is recommended, not obligatory, because modesty is already achieved by her clothes.

The Hanafi school places great emphasis on modesty, even in the context of punishment. This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies the ruling on digging a pit for a woman when she is being flogged. It explains that while the act is permissible and encouraged for additional modesty, it is not a requirement, as her clothing already fulfills the basic need for concealment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Legal Category
The act is digging a pit for a woman during flogging. The passage states it is permissible (ja'iz) and recommended (mustahab). Determine why it is not obligatory.
2
Analyse the Reason for Non-Obligation
The commentator points out that the word 'permissible' indicates it is not wajib. The purpose of digging is modesty, which is already achieved by her clothes. Extran modesty is why it is recommended.
3
Examine the Evidence
The proof comes from Sahih Muslim in the story of the Ghamidi woman who was stoned at the time of the Prophet, and similar reports from Ali in Ahmad and al-Bayhaqi. This shows the practice was done but not commanded.
The evidence is ahad, thus zanni, supporting recommendation rather than obligation.
4
Determine the Hanafi Position
The mufta bihi is that digging a pit for a woman up to her chest is mustahab (recommended). It is not compulsory, and the flogging is valid without it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجاز الحفر لها [2]قوله : وجاز الحفر لها ؛ أشار بذكر الجواز إلى أنه ليس بواجب، فإنّ الغرض من الحفر حصول الستر، وهو حاصل بثيابها، لكن لما كان فيه الستر الزائد استحب الحفر لها إلى صدرها، وقد ثبت ذلك في صحيح مسلم في رجم الغامدية في الزمان النبوي، وعن علي ثبت نحوه عند أحمد والبيهقي.
And digging for her is permissible. [2] He said: 'And digging for her is permissible'; he pointed by mentioning permissibility that it is not obligatory. Indeed, the purpose of digging is to achieve modesty, and modesty is achieved by her clothes, but because there is additional modesty in it, it is recommended to dig for her up to her chest. This has been established in Sahih Muslim in the story of stoning the Ghamidi woman at the time of the Prophet, and similar is reported from Ali in Ahmad and al-Bayhaqi.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage discusses whether a pit should be dug for a woman when she is being flogged (or stoned, as the hadith refers to stoning). The commentator clarifies that the mention of 'permissibility' (jawaz) indicates it is not obligatory; the basic modesty is already achieved by her clothes. However, digging a pit up to her chest provides extra modesty and is therefore recommended (mustahab). The proof comes from the hadith of the Ghamidi woman stoned in the Prophet's time. This shows the Hanafi position favoring the practice for women but not requiring it for men.

Key Terms
الحفر (al-hafr): Digging a pit in the ground for the woman to stand in during punishment to conceal her body up to the chest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Digging a pit for a woman during flogging is a recommended practice for extra modesty, not an obligation.

Ruling on Not Digging a Pit for a Man During Flogging
States that a pit is not dug for a man, with supporting hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on digging a pit for a man during flogging and cite the supporting hadith evidence, including how the conflicting narration is reconciled.

Core Idea
A pit is not dug for a man during hadd punishment because the prophetic practice with Ma'iz did not include it.

When implementing hadd punishments, the method must align with prophetic practice. One detail concerns whether a pit should be dug for the person being flogged or stoned. The Hanafi school holds that a pit is not dug for a man, based on the prophetic treatment of Ma'iz ibn Malik. This lesson examines the evidence and addresses a conflicting narration from Buraidah, showing how the Hanafi school resolves apparent contradictions in the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act under discussion is digging a pit for a man during flogging (or stoning). The Hanafi ruling is that it should not be done; it is makruh tanzih (disliked).
The ruling applies specifically to men; women have a different ruling.
2
Examine the primary evidence
The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri about Ma'iz ibn Malik is cited. In this hadith, the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered stoning, but no pit was dug. The narrator emphasizes 'we did not tie him nor dig a pit.' This absence indicates that the practice is not required.
This hadith is from Sahih Muslim, munqati'? Actually it's mursal? But treated as evidence.
3
Address the conflicting narration
There is a conflicting report from Buraidah that a pit was dug for Ma'iz up to his chest. This appears to contradict the primary evidence. The commentator presents both but must reconcile.
Both reports are from Muslim, so the conflict is in the same source.
4
Apply Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school gives preference to the version without a pit. The reasoning includes: (a) the absence of digging is the more common practice, (b) there is no need for a pit for a man as modesty is preserved otherwise, and (c) the majority of narrations support the simpler procedure. This does not mean the Buraidah report is rejected, but that the ruling is based on the weightier evidence.
Tarjih is done based on the principle of 'taking the more cautious and widely practiced'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا له [3]قوله : لا له ؛ أي لا يحفر للرجل ؛ لعدم الحاجة إليه، ويؤيده قول أبي سعيد الخدري ه: «لما أمر النبي برجم ماعز : خرجنا به إلى البقيع، فوالله ما أوثقناه ولا حفرنا، ولكنه قام للناس فرميناه بالعظام والمدرِ والخزف، فاشتد واشتددنا خلفه، أتى عرض الحرة فرميناه بجلاميد الحرّة حتى سكت» (۲) ، أخرجه مسلم، ويخالفه ما أخرجه مسلم ايضاً من رواية بريدة : إنّه حفر له حفرة إلى صدره (۳).
Not for him. [3] He said: 'Not for him'; meaning a pit is not dug for a man due to no need for it. This is supported by the statement of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: When the Prophet ordered the stoning of Ma'iz, we took him out to al-Baqi'. By Allah, we did not tie him nor dig a pit. He stood for the people and we threw at him with bones, clay, and pieces of pottery. He ran and we chased him until he reached the black stones, then we threw at him with lumps of black stone until he became silent. Narrated by Muslim. And it is contradicted by what Muslim also narrated on the authority of Buraidah: that a pit was dug for him to his chest.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that a pit is not dug for a man being punished. The commentator explains there is no need because a man's modesty concerns are different. The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri regarding Ma'iz ibn Malik is cited as evidence: the Prophet did not order a pit for him. However, a conflicting narration from Buraidah mentions a pit. The commentator presents both but implies the preferred view is that no pit is dug, as the dominant practice was without it.

Key Terms
لا له (la lahu): Expression meaning 'not for him', i.e., no pit is dug for the man.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is recommended
C) It is not done, as per the practice with Ma'iz
D) It is permissible but not recommended

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a pit should not be dug for a man during flogging or stoning, based on the stronger prophetic evidence from the story of Ma'iz.

The Ruling on Not Combining Flogging and Stoning for a Muhsan
Forbids combining flogging and stoning for the muhsan adulterer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on punishment for a muhsan adulterer and support it with the dalil from the Prophet's practice.

Core Idea
A muhsan adulterer is stoned, never flogged — the Prophet's practice is the proof.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the punishment for a married adulterer (muhsan). While some schools combine flogging and stoning, the Hanafi school holds that only stoning is prescribed. The proof lies in the consistent practice of the Prophet ﷺ, as recorded in multiple authentic hadith where only stoning was applied and flogging is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage carefully: 'flogging and stoning are not combined... rather stoning alone suffices.' What is the exact punishment for a muhsan?
The ruling is negative: no combination, so only stoning.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites three incidents: Ma'iz, the Ghamidi woman, and the woman whose hired man committed adultery. Identify what these have in common.
All are reported in Sahih Muslim and Sunan; flogging is not mentioned in any.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the absence of flogging in these reports become evidence? Consider that the Prophet consistently applied only stoning for muhsan offenders.
The evidence is the consistent practice (sunnah fi'liyyah) showing what the ruling is.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) combine flogging and stoning based on a hadith from 'Ubadah. How does the Hanafi school respond?
Hanafis hold that the later practice of the Prophet abrogates or specifies that hadith.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon in the Hanafi school? Why is this considered the stronger view?
The mufta bihi is stoning alone, based on zahir al-riwayah and multiple consistent reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جمع بين جلد ورجم [4]قوله : ولا جمع بين جلد ورجم ؛ أي لا يجمع بين الجلد والرجم للزاني المحصن، بل يكتفي بالرجم به وردت الأخبار، فقد ثبتت في الصحاح والسنن قصة رجم ماعز وقصة رجم المرأة الغامدية، وقصة رجم امرأة زنى بها أجير زوجها، ولم يرو الجلد في أحدها.
And there is no combining between flogging and stoning. [4] He said: 'And there is no combining between flogging and stoning'; meaning flogging and stoning are not combined for the muhsan adulterer. Rather, stoning alone suffices, as the reports have come. Indeed, in the authentic collections and sunan, the story of stoning Ma'iz, the story of stoning the Ghamidi woman, and the story of stoning the woman with whom her hired man committed adultery are established, and flogging is not reported in any of them.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This ruling states that the muhsan (married) adulterer is not subjected to both flogging and stoning. The commentator emphasizes that only stoning is applied, citing multiple incidents from the Prophet's time where only stoning occurred. The absence of flogging in these cases indicates that the punishment for muhsan is exclusively stoning, as per the Hanafi school. This is in contrast to some other schools that may combine.

Key Terms
المحصن (al-muhsan): A married person who committed adultery; eligible for stoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Stoning only
C) Both flogging and stoning
D) Banishment only

Key Takeaway
For a muhsan adulterer, the Hanafi school applies only stoning, based on the consistent Prophetic practice of not combining flogging.

Ruling on Not Combining Flogging and Banishment Except as Policy
States that flogging and banishment are not combined except as a discretionary policy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining flogging and banishment for a non-muhsan adulterer and explain the role of siyasah.

Core Idea
Flogging is the only fixed punishment; banishment can be added only by the ruler as a discretionary policy (siyasah).

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies the scope of punishment for a non-muhsan adulterer. The Quran specifies flogging, and the Hanafi school strictly limits the fixed penalty to that. However, the ruler retains the authority to impose additional measures like banishment for public good, but not as part of the hadd itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is zina by a non-muhsan (unmarried) person. The ruling is that flogging (jald) is the prescribed punishment (hadd), and banishment (nafy) is not part of it.
The ruling distinguishes between fixed hadd and discretionary siyasah.
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verse (Surah al-Nur 24:2) is cited. It commands flogging only.
The verse is the primary textual evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The silence of the verse regarding banishment indicates it is not part of the hadd. If it were, it would have been mentioned necessarily.
This is a Hanafi linguistic usul principle: silence on an issue indicates non-inclusion.
4
Note the exception 'illa siyasah'
The phrase 'except as policy' allows the ruler to add banishment as a discretionary measure (siyasah) for deterrence.
This exception is not part of the hadd but a separate authority.
5
Understand the definition of siyasah
Siyasah is defined as the ruler's actions for reform and good management, even without specific textual evidence, provided it is just and achieves Shari'ah objectives.
This defines the scope and limits of the ruler's discretionary authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is no combination between flogging and banishment except as policy. [1] He said: 'And there is no combination between flogging and banishment'; meaning for the non-muhsan adulterer, flogging and banishment are not combined, such as his expulsion from that town for a year to deter him. Because the punishment for the non-muhsan is mentioned in the verse, and it suffices with flogging. Had banishment been part of the punishment, it would necessarily have been mentioned in the verse. [2] He said: 'Except as policy': it is the infinitive of 'the ruler ruled the subjects': he commanded and forbade them. Its essence is the reformation of creation by guiding them to the saving path and good management. Some have defined it as intensifying the punishment for an offense that has a legal ruling to cut off the material of corruption. In al-Bahr: 'The apparent meaning of their words is that policy is something done by the ruler for a benefit he sees, even if there is no specific evidence for that action.' And al-Hamawi said: 'Policy is a strict law. It is of two types: oppressive policy, which the Shari'ah prohibits, and just policy, which extracts the truth from the oppressor, prevents many injustices, deters corrupt people, and achieves the objectives of the Shari'ah. So the Shari'ah requires recourse to it and reliance on it for the manifestation of the truth.' (End quote from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 5:11).

Commentary
This ruling addresses the punishment for a non-muhsan (unmarried) adulterer. The Hanafi school holds that the Quranic verse only specifies flogging (one hundred lashes), and banishment is not part of the prescribed punishment (hadd). Therefore, a judge cannot combine flogging with banishment as a matter of fixed penalty. However, the exception 'except as policy' (illa siyasah) allows the ruler, in his discretionary capacity, to add banishment for the sake of deterrence and public interest. The commentator defines 'siyasah' as the ruler's actions to reform society, even if specific textual evidence is absent. This is a crucial principle: the ruler can impose additional measures as 'siyasah' provided they are just and align with Shari'ah objectives.

Key Terms
السياسة (al-siyasah): The ruler's discretionary authority to impose additional punishments or measures for public benefit, even without specific textual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, never
C) Yes, but only as a discretionary policy (siyasah) by the ruler
D) Only if the judge deems it

Key Takeaway
Flogging is the only fixed punishment for non-muhsan zina; banishment is a discretionary measure (siyasah) that the ruler may impose for public welfare.

Combining Flogging and Exile for the Unmarried Adulterer
Dispute on whether exile is part of the hadd for non-muhsan or discretionary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on exile for the non-muhsan, identify the Shafi'i counterargument, and explain the Hanafi response based on abrogation and grammatical analysis.

Core Idea
The hadd for the non-muhsan is only flogging; exile is a discretionary policy, not a fixed punishment.

The punishment for adultery by an unmarried (non-muhsan) person is a point of disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Quran mentions only flogging, but the Sunnah includes reports of exile. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that exile is not part of the fixed hadd but rather a discretionary policy (siyasa) left to the ruler's judgment, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view that combines flogging and exile as an obligatory hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute is about whether the unmarried adulterer receives both flogging and exile as part of the fixed punishment (hadd) or whether exile is merely a discretionary measure.
2
State the Hanafi position and its primary evidence
According to the Hanafi school, the hadd is only flogging (as per Quran 24:2). Exile is not part of the hadd; it is a siyasa (policy) that the ruler may apply for deterrence.
The key phrase in the text: 'ولا بين جلد ونفي إلا سياسة'
3
Identify the Shafi'i evidence
The Shafi'i school relies on the hadith of 'Ubada ('The virgin with the virgin: 100 lashes and exile for a year') and a hadith in al-Bukhari where the Prophet ruled for a non-muhsan with exile and flogging.
These hadith explicitly combine both punishments.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) The hadith of 'Ubada is abrogated (mansukh). (2) In the other hadith, the conjunction 'and' (waw) indicates separation, meaning exile is not part of the same obligation. The Prophet's action was a discretionary policy, not a fixed ruling.
The text also mentions the practice of the caliphs and 'Ali's statement that exile is a fitna.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi fiqh, a judge may optionally add exile as a discretionary punishment but is not required to. In Shafi'i fiqh, exile is an obligatory part of the hadd for the unmarried.
This affects the judge's authority and the binding nature of the punishment.
