And there is no combining between flogging and exile except as a policy (siyasa). This is our position. And according to al-Shafi'i, he (the judge) combines for the virgin (unmarried) between flogging and exile. [1] His statement: 'And no combining between flogging and exile' means that for the fornicator who is not muhsan, flogging and exile are not combined such that he is expelled from that town for a year to be deterred, because the hadd of the non-muhsan is mentioned in the verse, and it sufficed with flogging. If exile were part of the hadd, it would have been mentioned in the verse necessarily. [2] His statement: 'except as policy (siyasa)': siyasa is derived from 'sasa' meaning the ruler commanded and forbade them; its essence is reforming the creation by guiding them to the saving path and good management. Some defined it as intensifying a transgression that has a legal ruling to cut off the root of corruption. And in al-Bahr: 'The apparent meaning of their words is that siyasa is an action done by the ruler for a maslaha he perceives, even if no specific textual evidence (dalil juz'i) exists for that action.' And al-Hamawi said: Siyasa is an intensified law (shar' mughallaz), and it is of two types: oppressive siyasa - the Shari'a prohibits it, and just siyasa - it extracts the truth from the oppressor and prevents many injustices, and deters people of corruption, and leads to the objectives of the Shari'a. Upon this is interpreted what al-Tirmidhi and others narrated: 'Abu Bakr and 'Umar flogged the fornicator and exiled him for a year.' It is supported by the statement of 'Ali: 'Exile is sufficient as a trial (fitna).' Narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq. In summary, there is no doubt about the establishment of exile (taghrib) from the Messenger of Allah and the senior companions in word and deed, but there is no evidence in these reports that it is part of the hadd, such that flogging alone would be only part of the hadd.

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi position on the punishment for the unmarried (non-muhsan) adulterer. The ruling is that flogging (jald) is the fixed hadd punishment as mentioned in the Quran (Surah al-Nur, verse 2), and exile (nafy) is not part of this hadd. The phrase 'except as policy (siyasa)' indicates that a ruler may, for public interest or deterrence, add exile as a discretionary measure, but it is not a required component of the hadd. The author contrasts this with the Shafi'i position, which holds that for the virgin (bikr) the hadd combines both flogging and exile. The Shafi'i evidence includes the hadith of 'Ubada ibn al-Samit and a hadith in al-Bukhari. The Hanafi response, supported by the jumhur (majority of scholars), argues that the hadith of 'Ubada is abrogated (mansukh), and the other hadith, when analyzed grammatically (by the use of 'and' showing conjunction), actually indicates that exile is separate from the hadd, so it was done as a policy and as a deterrent, not as an obligatory part of the punishment. This interpretation is reinforced by the actions of the early caliphs (Abu Bakr and 'Umar) who combined flogging and exile but also by 'Umar's later statement after an exile led to apostasy. The passage also provides a detailed definition of siyasa from the works al-Bahr and Hamawi, explaining it as a discretionary power of the ruler based on public interest (maslaha), even in the absence of specific textual evidence. The overall message is that while exile is practiced, it is not part of the fixed hadd, and the ruler's policy must be just.

Key Terms
سياسة (siyasa): A discretionary action by the ruler based on public interest, even without specific textual evidence, to deter corruption and reform society.
حد (hadd): A fixed, prescribed punishment mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah that cannot be increased or decreased.
نفي (nafy): Exile from the town or region, also called taghrib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The hadd for the non-muhsan is only flogging; exile is a discretionary policy (siyasa) based on the ruler's judgment, not a fixed punishment.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i: Is Banishment Part of Hadd for the Virgin?
Refutes Shafi'i view that banishment is part of hadd for virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on banishment (nafy) for the virgin zani, explain the Hanafi proofs, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Banishment is ta'zir, not hadd; only flogging is the fixed punishment.

This lesson examines a well-known dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the punishment for a virgin (bikr) who commits zina. The Shafi'is hold that banishment (nafy) is part of the hadd, while the Hanafis maintain it is a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). We will explore the textual evidence from both sides and understand the Hanafi methodology of harmonizing the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school says about the hadd for a virgin. The hadd is solely flogging; banishment is not part of it.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that banishment is combined with flogging as part of the hadd for a virgin. Note their primary evidence.
Imam al-Shafi'i cites the hadith of 'Ubada and a narration from al-Bukhari.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith where the Prophet reportedly ordered banishment along with flogging for a non-muhsan.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the hadith of 'Ubada is abrogated, and the conjunction (waw) in the other hadith indicates banishment is a separate action, not part of the hadd. They cite the practice of Abu Bakr and 'Umar, 'Ali's statement, and 'Umar's cessation of exile.
The Hanafi response harmonizes the verse (Q.24:2) with the sunnah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis do not impose banishment as a hadd; Shafi'is do. For Hanafis, banishment may be applied as ta'zir at the ruler's discretion.

Book of Hudud: Its Definition and Manner. According to al-Shafi‘i, he combines flogging and banishment for the virgin. [1] His statement: 'According to al-Shafi‘i...' etc.; he has the hadith of ‘Ubada which was mentioned earlier in the discussion of combining stoning and flogging, and it is supported by what al-Bukhari recorded: 'The Messenger of Allah decided concerning one who committed zina and was not muhsan: banishment for a year and establishment of the hadd upon him.' The majority responded that the hadith of ‘Ubada is abrogated as previously mentioned, and the mentioned hadith indicates that banishment is not included in the obligatory hadd due to the conjunction (waw) being separate, so it is interpreted that he did it as a deterrent and policy. Along these lines is what al-Tirmidhi and others narrated: 'Abu Bakr and ‘Umar flogged the adulterer and exiled him for a year.' It is supported by the saying of ‘Ali: 'Sufficient for fitna is banishment,' recorded by ‘Abd al-Razzaq. He also recorded from ‘Umar that he exiled Rabi‘a ibn Umayya ibn Khalaf for drinking, and he joined Heraclius and became Christian, so ‘Umar said: 'I will not exile any Muslim after him.' In summary, there is no doubt about the establishment of exile from the Messenger of Allah and senior Companions in word and deed, but there is no indication in these narrations that it is part of the hadd, such that flogging alone is part of the hadd. Therefore, it is better to make the hadd solely flogging as the verse indicates, and to consider banishment established by the sunnah as ta‘zir and policy. If the hadith combining stoning and flogging for the muhsan adulterer is interpreted similarly, there is no problem. Thus the claim of bigots that the Hanafis contradicted the authentic hadith on exile is refuted, and it becomes clear that the Hanafis acted upon both the verse and the sunnah, giving each its due. Whoever thinks that banishment is part of the hadd and what is mentioned in the verse is part of the hadd should bring clear evidence for that, and that is nearly impossible.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the punishment for zina committed by a virgin (bikr). The Shafi‘i school holds that the hadd for a virgin combines flogging and banishment (nafy), citing the hadith of ‘Ubada and a narration in al-Bukhari. The Hanafi response (the 'majority') argues that the hadith of ‘Ubada is abrogated, and the wording of the other hadith indicates that banishment is not part of the hadd because it is joined by the conjunction 'and' (waw), implying a separate act. Instead, banishment was done by the Prophet and caliphs as a discretionary punishment (ta‘zir) for policy reasons. Evidence includes the practice of Abu Bakr and ‘Umar, the statement of ‘Ali that banishment is itself a fitna, and the incident of ‘Umar ceasing exile after a Muslim apostatized. The Hanafis conclude that the hadd is only flogging, based on the Quranic verse (Q.24:2), while banishment is ta‘zir. They refute the accusation of rejecting authentic hadith, claiming instead to harmonize all texts.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes, such as zina.
نفي (nafy): Banishment or exile from one's homeland, considered as part of the hadd by Shafi‘is but as ta‘zir by Hanafis.
تغريب (taghrib): Exile or banishment, synonymous with nafy in this context.
تعزير (ta‘zir): A discretionary punishment imposed by the ruler for crimes not subject to hadd or qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is banishment (nafy) not considered part of the hadd for a virgin committing zina?
A) Because the hadith about banishment is weak
B) Because the Quranic verse only mentions flogging, and banishment is ta‘zir based on the actions of the caliphs
C) Because the Prophet never implemented banishment
D) Because banishment is only for the muhsan

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the hadd for a virgin is only flogging; banishment is a discretionary ta'zir, not a fixed part of the punishment.

Definition of Taghrib as Banishment
Clarifies that taghrib means exile, not imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taghrib in the context of the hadd for zina and distinguish its meaning from the nafy punishment for highway robbery.

Core Idea
Taghrib in zina means exile, not imprisonment; imprisonment applies only to highway robbery.

This passage clarifies a crucial distinction in Hanafi hudud law. The author corrects a common error among scholars who mistakenly equate nafy (banishment) with imprisonment (habs) in the context of the punishment for zina. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly applying the ta'zir punishment of banishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term
Locate the key term 'taghrib 'amm' in the passage. Note that the author equates it with 'nafy'.
The term is part of a commentary on a hadith regarding the punishment for zina.
2
Understand the Correction
The author explicitly states that interpreting nafy as imprisonment in this context is a mistake (khatā').
Some scholars had confused the two meanings; the author refutes this.
3
Compare with Highway Robbery Verse
The passage acknowledges that in the verse on highway robbers (Q.5:33), nafy has been interpreted as imprisonment by some scholars. But that does not apply to zina.
This distinction prevents cross-contamination of meanings between different hudud.
4
Formulate the Definition
State the correct definition: taghrib/nafy in the hadd for zina means expulsion from one's town to another, i.e., exile.
Imprisonment is a separate punishment (ta'zir) not to be confused with banishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وهو تغريب عام ؛ يريد به أن النفي وتغريب عام المذكور في رواياتِ الحديث معناهما واحد ، وهو الإخراج من بلدته إلى غيرها ، وتفسير النفي في هذا المقام بالحبس كما ذكره بعض المشايخ خطأ، نعم قد فسّر به النفي الوارد في آية حد قطاع الطريق ، وهي قوله : إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَادًا أَن يُقَتَلُوا أَوْ يُصَلَّبُوا أَوْ تُقَطَّعَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَأَرْجُلُهُم مِّنْ خِلَافٍ أَوْ يُنفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ.

Commentary
This passage defines a key term. The author clarifies that 'taghrib 'amm' (general banishment) is synonymous with 'nafy' (banishment), both meaning expulsion from one's hometown to another place. He corrects a common error among some scholars who interpreted 'nafy' in this context (the hadd for zina) as imprisonment (habs). The author notes that the interpretation of nafy as imprisonment is correct only for the verse on highway robbers (Q.5:33), where banishment is one of the prescribed punishments. In the context of zina, however, banishment means exile, not imprisonment.

Key Terms
تغريب عام (taghrib 'amm): General banishment: expulsion from one's town to another as a punishment.
نفي (nafy): Banishment, synonymous with taghrib in this context.
حبس (habs): Imprisonment, a different punishment from banishment.

Stoning a Sick Person and Postponing Flogging
Ruling on implementing hudud on a sick adulterer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a sick muhsan adulterer is stoned immediately but flogging is postponed, and apply the principle of maqasid al-shar' in hudud.

Core Idea
Rajm aims to kill, illness assists; jald aims to deter, illness risks unintended death.

In this passage, the commentator explains a nuance in the implementation of hadd punishments for zina. While both rajm and jald are obligatory, the rule for a sick person differs depending on the purpose of the punishment. We explore the rational basis (ta'lil) behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two punishments
Rajm (stoning) and jald (flogging) are the hadd punishments for zina, applied to muhsan and non-muhsan respectively.
2
State the ruling for a sick person
The matn rules: a sick person who committed zina is stoned (if muhsan) but not flogged until he recovers.
3
Analyse the purpose of each punishment
The commentator explains: rajm is intended to kill (ihlak), jald is for deterrence (zajr) and causing pain (ilam), not death.
4
Apply the purpose to the condition of illness
Since rajm aims to kill, illness assists the purpose; for jald, illness risks death, which is not intended, so it is postponed.
5
Formulate the general principle
A punishment aimed at death is carried out even on a sick person; a punishment not aimed at death is postponed if there is a risk of death.
This is a qiyas based on the illa of purpose (maqsad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ويرجم مريض ؛ لأنّ المقصود بالرجم إهلاكه، فلا يقدر رجمه في مرضه، بل يعين على المقصود بخلاف الجلد، فإنّه ليس الغرض فيه الإهلاك، بل مجرد الزجر والإيلام، وفي جلد المريض احتمال لهلاكه فيوقف جلده إلى أن يصح، ويزول سقمه وضعفه.
His statement: 'And a sick person who committed zina is stoned'; because the purpose of stoning is to kill him, so his illness does not prevent stoning, rather it assists in achieving the purpose. Unlike flogging, for its purpose is not killing but merely deterrence and causing pain, and flogging a sick person carries the risk of his death, so his flogging is postponed until he recovers and his sickness and weakness are gone.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling regarding the implementation of hadd on a sick person. The matn states that a sick person who committed zina is stoned (if muhsan) but not flogged until he recovers. The reasoning (ta'lil) is based on the different objectives of stoning (rajm) and flogging (jald). Stoning is intended to cause death, so executing it on a sick person actually assists the objective. Flogging, however, is not intended to kill but to deter and cause pain; flogging a sick person might lead to his death unintentionally, so it is delayed until after recovery. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the purposes of the punishments.

Key Terms
رجم (rajm): Stoning to death, the hadd for a muhsan (married) adulterer.
جلد (jald): Flogging, the hadd for a non-muhsan (unmarried) adulterer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sick muhsan adulterer stoned immediately but his flogging postponed?
A) Because stoning is a more severe punishment
B) Because stoning aims to kill, which is assisted by illness, while flogging aims only to deter and might kill accidentally
C) Because flogging is not a hadd punishment
D) Because the sick person is not considered a muhsan

Key Takeaway
Rajm is immediate for a sick muhsan because illness assists its lethal objective; jald is postponed because illness risks unintended death, contrary to its deterrent purpose.

Postponing Punishment for a Pregnant Adulteress
Ruling on postponing stoning for a pregnant adulteress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on postponing stoning and flogging for a pregnant woman, and distinguish between imprisonment conditions based on proof type.

Core Idea
The unborn child's life is protected; execution is delayed until after delivery.

This ruling addresses a pregnant woman who committed zina and is liable to stoning. The Hanafi school prioritizes the life of the innocent fetus, postponing execution until after delivery. A further distinction is made regarding imprisonment before delivery depending on how her guilt was proven.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text states that the woman is stoned 'when she delivers', not before, and flogging is also postponed. This is the primary hukm regarding timing of punishment.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given is that harming the fetus would lead to its destruction, and the fetus is a sacred soul (nafs muhtaramah) with no crime. This rational basis (dalil aqli) overrides the general principle of immediate execution.
3
Apply the imprisonment distinction
The commentary adds a condition: if zina is proven by testimony (shahada), she is imprisoned until delivery to prevent escape. If proven by confession (iqrar), she is not imprisoned because she could retract her confession.
4
Recognize the legal principle
The ruling illustrates the Hanafi use of istihsan (juristic preference) to protect innocent life, and the importance of proof type in determining detention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ترجم حين وضعت ؛ لا قبل الوضع، ولا تجلد قبل الوضع كيلا يؤدّي إلى هلاكِ الولد ؛ لأنه نفس محترمة، لا جريمة منه، نعم تحبس إلى أن تلد إن كان الحد
(۱) لأن جنينها لا يستحق الرجم لعدم الجناية منه، وتحبس حتى تلد إن ثبت زناها بالشهادة، ولا تحبس إن ثبت بالإقرار . ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(۳ : ۲۱۲).
His statement: 'She is stoned when she delivers'; not before delivery, and she is not flogged before delivery so that it does not lead to the destruction of the child, because it is a sanctified soul, with no crime from it. Yes, she is imprisoned until she delivers if the hadd is established by testimony; if established by confession, she is not imprisoned. (Footnote: Because her fetus does not deserve stoning due to no crime from it, and she is imprisoned until delivery if her zina is proven by testimony, but not if proven by confession. See: Fath Bab al-Inaya 3:212.)
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a pregnant woman who committed zina and is liable to stoning (if muhsan). The ruling is that her punishment is postponed until after she gives birth. The reason given is that harming the fetus would be unjust, as the fetus is an innocent life (nafs muhtaramah) with no crime. Flogging is also postponed for the same reason. The commentary adds a distinction regarding imprisonment: if her zina is proven by testimony (shahada), she is imprisoned until delivery to prevent escape; but if proven by confession (iqrar), she is not imprisoned because she could retract her confession. This is based on the principle of preserving the life of the unborn child.

Key Terms
نفس محترمة (nafs muhtaramah): A sacred/honored life that is protected by law; here referring to the fetus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Dispute on Whether Banishment is Part of Hadd Zina
Arguing that banishment (nafy) is ta'zir not hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on banishment in zina, identify the Shafi'i opposing view, and explain how Hanafis harmonize Quran and hadith to classify banishment as ta'zir.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, banishment for zina is ta'zir, not hadd—the Quran alone defines the fixed punishment.

This lesson examines a sharp inter-madhhab disagreement: is banishment (nafy/taghrib) an integral part of the fixed punishment (hadd) for a muhsan (married person) who commits zina, or is it a discretionary ta'zir? The Hanafi school argues that the Quranic verse (24:2) prescribes only flogging, and the hadiths mentioning exile are understood as ta'zir. This passage refutes accusations that Hanafis reject authentic hadiths, showing instead how they harmonize the primary sources.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding banishment for zina. The commentator states the optimal view is to make the hadd only flogging as the verse indicates, and banishment is ta'zir and policy.
The verse referred to is Quran 24:2.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i and others consider banishment as part of the hadd for a muhsan. Their evidence is hadiths that combine stoning and exile.
The text refers to 'fanatics' who accuse Hanafis of rejecting authentic hadiths.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i dalil: hadiths such as 'the married man with the married woman: one hundred lashes, stoning, and exile for a year.'
The commentator implies these hadiths are authentic but interpreted differently.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school rebuts: they treat the Quranic verse as primary for the fixed hadd, and relegate the hadiths to ta'zir (discretionary punishment) operated as state policy (siyasah). They give each evidence its proper weight.
The commentator calls the opponent's demand for proof 'extremely difficult'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical difference: in Hanafi fiqh, a judge may or may not impose banishment as ta'zir; in Shafi'i fiqh, banishment is a mandatory component of the hadd.
This affects the punishment's fixedness and discretion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو تغريب عام.
فالأولى أن يجعل الحد هو الجلد وحده كما دلّت عليه الآية، ويجعل النفي الثابت بالسنن تعزيزاً وسياسة.
ولو حمل عليها حديث الجمع بين الرجم والجلد للزاني المحصن أيضاً لم يكن فيه بعد، فبطل قول المتعصبين : إنّ الحنفية خالفوا الأحاديث الصحيحة الواردة في التغريب، وظهر أنّ الحنفية قد عملوا في هذا الباب بكل من الآية والسنة، ووفروا حظ كل منهما على ما يُناسبه، ومن ظن أن النفي جزء الحد، وأن المذكور في الآية بعض الحد فليأتِ بدليل صريح يدلّ على ذلك، ودونه خرط القتاد.
(1) قوله : وهو تغريب عام ؛ يريد به أن النفي وتغريب عام المذكور في رواياتِ الحديث معناهما ،واحد ، وهو الإخراج من بلدته إلى غيرها ، وتفسير النفي في هذا المقام بالحبس كما ذكره بعض المشايخ خطأ، نعم قد فسّر به النفي الوارد في آية حد قطاع الطريق ، وهي قوله : إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَادًا أَن يُقَتَلُوا أَوْ يُصَلَّبُوا أَوْ تُقَطَّعَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَأَرْجُلُهُم مِّنْ خِلَافٍ أَوْ يُنفَوْا مِن الْأَرْضِ (٢).
It is general exile. The optimal view is to make the hadd only flogging as the verse indicates, and to make the banishment established by the sunnah as ta'zir and policy. If one were to interpret the hadith combining stoning and flogging for the muhsan also [as applying], there is no harm. Thus the statement of the fanatics that the Hanafis have opposed the authentic hadiths on exile is false, and it becomes apparent that the Hanafis have acted in this chapter according to both the verse and the sunnah, giving each its due. Whoever thinks that banishment is part of the hadd and that what is mentioned in the verse is part of the hadd, let him bring clear proof indicating that, and that is extremely difficult. (1) His statement: 'and it is general exile' means that banishment and general exile mentioned in the narrations of the hadith are one in meaning, which is expulsion from his town to another. The interpretation of banishment in this context as imprisonment, as some scholars have mentioned, is an error. Yes, it has been interpreted as such for the banishment mentioned in the verse of the punishment of highway robbers, which is His statement: 'Indeed, the penalty for those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger and strive upon earth [to cause] corruption is that they be killed or crucified or that their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides or that they be banished from the land' (5:33).
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The commentator begins by clarifying the meaning of 'general exile' mentioned in the matn. He states that nafy (banishment) and taghrib (exile) are synonymous: expulsion from one's town. Some scholars mistakenly interpreted nafy here as imprisonment, but that interpretation applies only to the verse on highway robbery (5:33). The core argument is that the Hanafi school considers the fixed punishment (hadd) for zina to be only flogging, as derived from Surah An-Nur:2. The banishment mentioned in hadith is not part of the hadd but rather a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) or policy (siyasah). The commentator refutes the accusation that Hanafis reject authentic hadiths on taghrib. Instead, Hanafis harmonize both the Quranic verse and the prophetic traditions by treating flogging as the prescribed hadd and keeping banishment as a supplementary measure. He challenges opponents to provide explicit proof that banishment is part of the fixed hadd, calling it an extremely difficult task. The passage defends the Hanafi methodology of combining textual evidences.

Key Terms
نفي (nafy): In this context, banishment or exile from one's town as a punishment for zina.
تغريب (taghrib): Exile, general exile; synonymous with nafy in this context.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge; not a fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, banishment for zina is a discretionary ta'zir, not part of the fixed hadd, harmonizing Quranic and hadith evidence.

Postponement of Hadd for the Sick and Pregnant: Rulings and Proof
Explaining rulings on delaying execution of hadd for sick and pregnant persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on delaying execution of hadd for a sick adulterer and a pregnant adulteress, and explain the proof from the hadith of Ghamidiyyah.

Core Idea
The hadd is delayed for pregnancy to protect the child; for sickness, only flogging is delayed to avoid death.

This lesson examines two specific rulings from the Hanafi school regarding the execution of hudud punishments. When a convicted adulterer is sick or pregnant, the timing of the punishment is adjusted to prevent harm—death of the person from flogging or death of the unborn child. The commentary explains the rationale and provides the key proof from the hadith of Ghamidiyyah. We will analyze the rulings and the hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases and their specific rulings
From the passage, extract the rule for a sick person (stoned, but flogging postponed until recovery) and for a pregnant woman (stoning and flogging postponed until after delivery).
Note the distinction: stoning's purpose is killing, so no delay; flogging's purpose is deterrence, so delay is possible to avoid causing death.
2
Understand the rationale behind each ruling
Analyze why flogging is delayed for a sick person (risk of death) and why both punishments are delayed for a pregnant woman (to protect the unborn child's life).
The child is innocent and must not be harmed by the punishment.
3
Note the imprisonment distinction between testimony and confession
If the hadd is proven by testimony (bayyinah), the woman is imprisoned until delivery to prevent flight. If proven by confession (iqrar), she is not imprisoned because she can retract her confession.
Confession can be retracted before execution, making imprisonment pointless.
4
Examine the hadith of Ghamidiyyah as the primary proof
The passage quotes two narrations from Sahih Muslim. The first narration indicates stoning after weaning, but the second (supported by 'Umran ibn Husayn) indicates stoning after delivery. The commentator notes that the first has a criticized narrator, while the second is stronger.
Thus the relied-upon ruling is that stoning occurs after delivery, not after weaning.
5
Derive the final Hanafi position
Combine the rulings and the hadith evidence to state the mufta bihi: a pregnant woman is stoned after delivery, and a sick person is stoned immediately but flogged after recovery.
This is the position of the Hanafi school as found in al-Hidayah and al-Fath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : ويرجم مريض ؛ لأنّ المقصود بالرجم إهلاكه، فلا يقدر رجمه في مرضه، بل يعين على المقصود بخلاف الجلد، فإنّه ليس الغرض فيه الإهلاك، بل مجرد الزجر والإيلام، وفي جلد المريض احتمال لهلاكه فيوقف جلده إلى أن يصح، ويزول سقمه وضعفه.
[٣] قوله : ترجم حين وضعت ؛ لا قبل الوضع، ولا تجلد قبل الوضع كيلا يؤدّي إلى هلاكِ الولد ؛ لأنه نفس محترمة، لا جريمة منه، نعم تحبس إلى أن تلد إن كان الحد ثابتاً بالبينة، كيلا تهرب، بخلاف الإقرار، فإنّ المقرّة لا تحبس ؛ لأنّ الرجوع عن الإقرار معتبر، فلا يفيد الحبس. كذا في «الهداية» و«الفتح»).
والأصل في هذا الباب حديث الغامدية، وقد اختلفت الرواية فيه، قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «تخريج أحاديث الهداية» في «صحيح مسلم»: «عن بشير بن المهاجر عن عبد الله بن بريدة عن أبيه قال: جاءت الغامدية فقالت : يا رسول الله ، إني زنيت فطهرني، وإنّه ردّها ، فلما كان الغد قالت : يا رسول الله لعلك تريد أن تردني كما رددت ماعزاً ، فوالله إنِّي لحبلى، فقال : أمّا لا ، فاذهبي حتى تلدي، فلما ولدت أتته بالصبي في يده كسرة خبز، فقالت يا رسول الله ، قد فطمته وقد أكل الطعام، فدفع الصبي إلى رجل من المسلمين، ثم أمر بها فحفر لها إلى صدرها ، وأمر الناس فرجموها). وأخرجه أيضاً عن علقمة بن مرثد عن سليمان بن بريدة عن أبيه، فذكره قال :
:
ويحك ارجعي فاستغفري الله ، وتوبي إليه قالت أراك تريد أن تردني كما رددت ما عزاً ، قال : وما ذاك، قالت إنّها حبلى من الزنا، قال: أنت، قالت: نعم، فقال لها : اذهبي حتى تضعي ما في بطنك ، قال : فكفلها رجل من الأنصار، ثم أتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : قد وضعت الغامدية ، قال : إذاً لا نرجمها وندع ولدها صغيراً ليس لـه مـن ير ضعه، فقال رجل من الأنصار : فقال : إليّ رضاعه ، قال : فرجمها (۳).
وفي هذا ما يقتضي أنه رجمها حين وضعت وفي الأوّل ما يقتضي أنه تركها حتى فطمت ولدها، ولكنّ الأوّل فيه بشير بن المهاجر وفيه مقال، ويتقوى الثاني برواية عمران بن حصين، أخرجها مسلم أيضاً، وفيها : إنه رجمها بعد أن وضعت»،
[2] His statement: 'and a sick person who committed zina is stoned' because the purpose of stoning is to kill him, so he is not stoned in his illness; rather it helps the purpose. Unlike flogging, for the purpose is not killing but mere deterrence and infliction of pain. In flogging a sick person, there is possibility of his death, so his flogging is suspended until he recovers and his illness and weakness are gone. [3] His statement: 'a pregnant woman who committed zina is stoned upon her delivery' i.e., not before delivery, and she is not flogged before delivery lest it lead to the death of the child, because it is a respected soul with no crime. Rather, she is imprisoned until she gives birth if the hadd is established by testimony, to prevent her from fleeing. This is contrary to the case of confession, for the woman who confesses is not imprisoned, because retraction of confession is considered, so imprisonment would be futile. This is stated in al-Hidayah and al-Fath. The source in this chapter is the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah, and the narration differs concerning it. al-Zayla'i in Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah says in Sahih Muslim: from Bashir ibn al-Muhajir from 'Abdullah ibn Buraydah from his father, who said: 'al-Ghamidiyyah came and said: O Messenger of Allah, I have committed zina, so purify me. He sent her back. The next day she said: O Messenger of Allah, perhaps you want to send me back as you sent back Ma'iz? By Allah, I am pregnant. He said: If so, go until you give birth. When she gave birth, she came with the baby in her hand holding a piece of bread, and said: O Messenger of Allah, I have weaned him and he has eaten food. He then gave the boy to a man from the Muslims, ordered that a pit be dug for her up to her chest, and ordered the people to stone her.' Another narration from 'Alqamah ibn Marthad from Sulayman ibn Buraydah from his father: he mentioned it. He said: 'Woe to you, go back and seek Allah's forgiveness and repent to Him.' She said: 'I see you want to send me back as you sent back Ma'iz.' He said: 'What is that?' She said: 'She is pregnant from zina.' He said: 'You?' She said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Go until you deliver what is in your womb.' He said: 'So a man from the Ansar took responsibility for her. Then he came to the Prophet (SAW) and said: 'al-Ghamidiyyah has delivered.' He said: 'Then we will not stone her and leave her child small with no one to breastfeed him.' A man from the Ansar said: 'I will take care of his breastfeeding.' He said: 'So he stoned her.' This narration indicates that he stoned her when she delivered, while the first narration indicates that he left her until she weaned her child. However, the first narration includes Bashir ibn al-Muhajir, who is criticised. The second narration is strengthened by the narration of 'Umran ibn Husayn, also narrated by Muslim, in which it says: 'He stoned her after she had delivered.'
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The commentator explains two rulings derived from the matn: first, a sick person who committed zina is not flogged until he recovers, because the purpose of flogging is deterrence and not killing; flogging a sick person may cause death, so it is delayed. However, stoning is still carried out because its purpose is death, and delaying it would not serve the purpose. Second, a pregnant woman who committed zina is not stoned or flogged until she delivers, to protect the life of the unborn child. If the hadd is proven by testimony (bayanah), she is imprisoned until delivery to prevent flight; if proven by confession (iqrar), she is not imprisoned because she can retract her confession. The commentator then provides the proof: the famous hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah. He notes two different narrations: one where she was stoned right after delivery, and another where she was stoned after weaning the child. He analyzes the chains: the first narration has Bashir ibn al-Muhajir who is criticised, while the second is supported by the narration of 'Umran ibn Husayn, which is in Sahih Muslim. The stronger evidence supports that stoning occurs after delivery, not after weaning. This shows the Hanafi reliance on authentic hadith and careful evaluation of narrations.

Key Terms
رجم (rajm): Stoning to death as a punishment for adultery (zina) for a muhsan (married person).
جلد (jald): Flogging as a punishment for zina for non-muhsan (unmarried person).
بينة (bayyinah): Testimony, legal evidence of the crime by witnesses.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession of the crime by the accused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling discussed, what happens to a sick adulterer (muhsan) who is sentenced to rajm and jald?
A) He is stoned immediately and flogged after recovery
B) He is not stoned until he recovers
C) He is flogged immediately but stoning is delayed
D) He is neither stoned nor flogged until recovery

Key Takeaway
The hadd is obligatory but its execution is postponed for pregnancy and for flogging in sickness to prevent harm; the hadith of Ghamidiyyah provides the primary evidence.

Imprisonment of Adulteress and Timing of Stoning for Child Welfare
Discusses imprisonment based on evidence vs confession and timing of stoning for pregnant adulteress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on imprisonment of an adulteress based on evidence vs confession, and explain the timing of stoning for a pregnant or nursing adulteress, citing the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah.

Core Idea
A confessing adulteress is not imprisoned; stoning is delayed if the child has no caregiver.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses two key procedural rulings in Hanafi hudud: first, whether an adulteress should be imprisoned before execution, and second, when stoning should be carried out for a pregnant or nursing woman. The distinction hinges on the mode of proof—evidence (bayyinah) vs confession—and the welfare of the child. The hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah provides the primary evidence, with multiple narrations that require careful harmonization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage opens by contrasting a woman proven by evidence (bayyinah) and a woman who confesses (muqirrah). State the ruling for each.
Imprisonment is for the one proven by evidence to prevent escape; the confessor is not imprisoned because retraction is accepted.
2
Examine the primary evidence
The basis is the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah. Note that there are multiple narrations differing on whether stoning occurred after birth or after weaning.
Al-Zayla'i in Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah cites two chains: one through Bashir ibn al-Muhajir (weak) and another through Imran ibn Husayn (stronger).
3
Understand the reconciliation
Some scholars reconcile the narrations by positing two different women—one who had a caregiver for her child and one who did not.
This yields the principle: stoning is delayed if the child has no one to care for it.
4
Apply the ruling on postpartum bleeding
The passage ends with 'after nifas' meaning after postpartum bleeding stops, as it is a type of illness, so execution awaits recovery.
This ensures the woman is in a state to undergo the punishment.

Established by evidence, so that she does not flee, contrary to confession – for the female confessor is not imprisoned because retraction of confession is considered, so imprisonment is not beneficial. Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Fath. The basis in this chapter is the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah, and the narration differs regarding it. And he sent her back. The next day she said: O Messenger of Allah, perhaps you intend to send me back as you sent back Ma'iz? By Allah, I am pregnant. He said: If not, then go until you give birth. So he handed the child over to a man from the Muslims, then ordered a hole to be dug for her up to her chest and ordered the people to stone her.' In this is indicated that he stoned her when she gave birth, and in the first it indicates that he left her until she weaned her child. But in the first there is Bashir ibn al-Muhajir and there is criticism of him. And the second is strengthened by the narration of Imran ibn Husayn, also narrated by Muslim, in which he stoned her after she gave birth. Some said: It is possible that they were two women: one found a guarantor for her child, and the other did not find a guarantor for her child, so her mother became obligatory for her until her child becomes independent.' End. His saying: 'after postpartum bleeding' – meaning after it ceases, because it is a type of illness, so one waits until she recovers from it.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addressing the rulings on stoning (rajm) for adultery and the treatment of the female offender. The author begins by contrasting two scenarios: a woman whose adultery is proven by evidence (bayyinah) is imprisoned to prevent escape, whereas a woman who confesses is not imprisoned because Hanafi law allows retraction of a confession, making imprisonment pointless. The basis for the stoning procedure is the famous hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah, transmitted in multiple versions in Sahih Muslim. The core issue is the timing of stoning: should it be immediately after childbirth or only after the child is weaned? Two narrations are presented, one through Bashir ibn al-Muhajir (criticized as weak) and another through Imran ibn Husayn (stronger). The stronger narration indicates stoning after birth, but the author notes that some scholars reconcile them by positing two different women: one for whom a wet nurse was available (stoned after birth) and another for whom no caregiver existed (delayed until weaning). The passage concludes by explaining the term 'after postpartum bleeding' (ba'd al-nifas): it means after the bleeding stops, as it is a kind of illness, so execution awaits recovery. This careful discussion shows the Hanafi method of harmonizing conflicting primary texts (ta'wil) and prioritizing stronger narrations.

Key Terms
الغامدية (al-Ghamidiyyah): A woman from the tribe of Ghamid who confessed to adultery before the Prophet ﷺ and was stoned after giving birth and weaning her child.
مقرّة (muqirrah): A female who confesses to a crime (here, adultery) before the court.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, specifically the testimony of four male witnesses in adultery cases.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding following childbirth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because women are never imprisoned in Islamic law.
D) Because the confessor must first be stoned.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between evidence (imprisonment) and confession (no imprisonment) in hudud, and delays stoning for a nursing mother if the child lacks a caregiver, prioritizing child welfare.

Shubha Averts Hadd: Definition and Proof
Definition and proof of the principle that shubha averts hadd punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shubha, explain its effect on hadd punishments, and cite the primary hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Shubha (legal ambiguity) repels hadd punishment – doubt requires suspension of the fixed penalty.

In this passage, the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines the key term shubha and establishes the fundamental Hanafi principle that hadd punishments are averted in cases of doubt. The proof is the prophetic command to avert hadd due to ambiguities. This principle is central to the Hanafi approach to criminal law, ensuring that punishments are not applied when there is genuine uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Define shubha linguistically
Read the author's definition: shubha is that which resembles the established truth but is not actually established. Note the root and meaning.
2
Understand 'dar'iah'
The author explains dar'iah comes from dar' meaning to repel. Shubha is the repeller of hadd.
3
Identify the primary evidence
The hadith 'Adra'u al-hudud bi al-shubahat' is the basis. Confirm its transmission via Imam Abu Hanifah.
4
Analyze the legal implication
The imperative 'idra'u' indicates obligation; shubha triggers a wajib duty to avert hadd.
5
Consider supporting narrations
Additional chains from Ali and Abu Hurairah reinforce that hadd should be warded off as long as a means exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوطء الذي يوجب الحد أو لا (الشبهة دارئة للحد). قوله : الشبهة هي بالضم ما يشبه الثابت وليس بثابت في نفس الأمر. قوله : دارئة اسم فاعل من الدرء بالفتح بمعنى الدفع. والأصل في هذا الباب حديث : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات أخرجه الإمام أبو حنيفة عن مقسم عن ابن عباس عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كما في مسند أبي حنيفة الذي جمعه الحصكفي والمسند الذي جمعه الخوارزمي وغيرهما. وفي رواية الدار قطني والبيهقي عن علي مرفوعا: ادرؤوا الحدود ولا ينبغي للإمام أن يعطل الحدود. وفي رواية ابن ماجة عن أبي هريرة مرفوعا: ادفعوا الحدود عن عباد الله ما وجدتم له مدفعا.
Chapter of Intercourse that Necessitates Hadd or Not (Legal ambiguity averts hadd). He said: Ash-Shubhah (ambiguity) is, with dammah, that which resembles the established but is not established in reality. He said: Dāri'ah is a noun of agent from ad-dar' (with fathah), meaning repelling. And the basis in this chapter is the hadith: 'Avert the hadd penalties due to ambiguities,' narrated by Imam Abu Hanifah from Miqsam from Ibn Abbas from the Prophet, peace be upon him, as in the Musnad of Abu Hanifah compiled by al-Haskafi and the Musnad compiled by al-Khawarizmi and others. And in a narration of ad-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi from Ali, may God be pleased with him, raised: 'Avert the hadd penalties, and it is not befitting for the ruler to suspend the hadd penalties.' And in a narration of Ibn Majah from Abu Hurairah, may God be pleased with him, raised: 'Ward off the hadd penalties from God's servants as long as you find a means to ward them off.'
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: 'Shubha averts hadd.' The author linguistically analyzes 'shubha' as something resembling the established truth but not actually established, and 'dāri'ah' as a repeller. The proof is a hadith reported via different chains: the Prophet commanded that hadd punishments be averted due to ambiguities. The Hanafi school relies on this principle to suspend hadd punishments in cases of doubt, such as when ownership is uncertain or consent is ambiguous. The hadith is transmitted by Imam Abu Hanifah himself, giving it strong authority within the school. The additional narrations from Ali and Abu Hurairah reinforce the principle and add that the ruler should not arbitrarily suspend punishments—only when there is valid shubha.

Key Terms
الشبهة (ash-shubhah): That which resembles the established truth but is not actually established in reality; a legal ambiguity that creates doubt about the applicability of a hadd punishment.
دارئة (dāri'ah): A noun of agent meaning 'repeller' or 'averter'; here it functions as a predicate indicating that shubha repels the hadd punishment.
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed, divinely ordained punishment for specific crimes such as zina, theft, and drinking alcohol; as opposed to ta'zir (discretionary punishment).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated in this passage, what is the effect of shubha (ambiguity) on a hadd punishment?
A) It increases the hadd punishment.
B) It has no effect on the hadd punishment.
C) It averts or prevents the hadd punishment.
D) It changes the hadd to a ta'zir punishment.

Key Takeaway
Shubha (legal ambiguity) repels hadd punishments; this is wajib based on the prophetic command and is a cornerstone of Hanafi criminal law.

The Maxim: Doubt Averts Hadd Punishment and Its Classification
Defining the legal maxim and classifying types of doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on doubt averting hadd, identify its hadith proof, and classify the types of shubha recognized in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The maxim directs judges to avert fixed punishments when any legally recognized doubt exists.

The hadd punishments are fixed by divine law, but their application is not mechanical. The Hanafi school, following a Prophetic command, instructs judges to avoid applying hadd whenever there is a plausible doubt that might indicate the act was not criminally liable. This lesson examines the foundational maxim, its textual evidence, and the classification of doubt (shubha) that triggers its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its meaning
Understand the phrase 'al-shubha dāri'a li-l-hadd' and the terminological definitions of shubha (that which resembles the truth but is not) and dāri'a (repelling).
The maxim is a guiding principle for judges, not a standalone ruling.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith 'Adra'u al-hudūd bi al-shubahāt' (Avert the hudud by doubts) reported through multiple chains. Note that the imperative indicates a command to avoid application when doubt exists.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning (dalala) is clear.
3
Classify shubha
The text explicitly divides shubha into two types: shubha fi al-fi'l (in the act) and shubha fi al-mahal (in the subject). Understand the distinction: shubha fi al-mahal is objective and independent of the offender's belief; shubha fi al-fi'l is subjective and specific to the individual.
A third type, shubhat al-'aqd, is mentioned as specific to Abu Hanifa.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Hanifa recognizes shubhat al-'aqd (doubt in the contract) as sufficient to avert hadd, but his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, do not. This ikhtilaf affects the scope of the maxim in cases of defective marriages.
The commentator notes this disagreement but does not resolve it definitively.

Source Text

وفي رواية ابن أبي شَيْبَة والتِّرْمِذِي والحاكم والبيهقي عن . عائشة رضي الله عنها مرفوعاً: «ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم ، فإن وجدتم للمسلم مخرجاً فخلوا سبيله، فإنّ الإمام لأن يخطئ في العفو خير له من أن يخطئ في العقوبة). وفي الباب آثار كثيرة بسطتها في رسالتي : القول الجازم بسقوط الحد بنكاح المحارم»، وهي رسالة فريدة في بابها ، مَن شاءَ الاطّلاع على نفائس شريفة فليطالعها. Doubt is a preventive of the hadd punishment. Chapter on intercourse that necessitates or does not necessitate hadd. ("Doubt is a preventive of the hadd.") [1] His statement: "al-shubha" – it is with damm – that which resembles the established, while it is not established in actuality. [2] His statement: "dāri'a" – is the active participle from al-dar' – with fat'h – meaning repelling, and the basis in this chapter is the hadith: "Avert the hudud by doubts," extracted by Imam Abu Hanifa from Miqsam from Ibn 'Abbas from the Prophet, as in the Musnad of Abu Hanifa compiled by al-Haskafi, and the Musnad compiled by al-Khwarizmi, and others. In the narration of al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi from 'Ali marfu'an: "Avert the hudud, and it is not befitting for the imam to suspend the hudud." In the narration of Ibn Majah from Abu Hurayra marfu'an: "Repel the hudud from the servants of Allah as long as you find a means of repelling." (Footnotes: references to sources and biographical notes on narrators.) Know that doubt (shubha) is of two types: 1. In the act, and 2. In the subject." In the narration of Ibn Abi Shayba, al-Tirmidhi, al-Hakim, and al-Bayhaqi from 'A'isha marfu'an: "Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if you find a way out for a Muslim, let him go, for the imam erring in pardon is better than him erring in punishment." And there are many reports in this chapter, which I have expanded in my treatise: "al-Qawl al-Jāzim bi Suqūt al-Hadd bi Nikāh al-Mahārim" – a unique treatise in its topic; whoever wishes to see noble gems should consult it. [1] His statement: "darbān" i.e. two divisions, and there is a third division called shubhat al-'aqd according to Abu Hanifa, but he did not mention it because it is not agreed upon, for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not consider marriage to be a doubt that drops the hadd, as will come in detail, God willing. And it is possible that he did not mention it because it is subsumed under one of the two divisions, for some include shubhat al-'aqd under shubha in the act, and some include it under shubha in the subject. [2] His statement: "in the act and in the subject"; shubha in the subject: i.e. the subject of intercourse, which is the woman, and it is also called effective doubt (shubha hukmiyya) and doubt in ownership. It is that there exists evidence negating prohibition in the subject in actuality, without depending on the criminal's assumption or belief, thus causing confusion, even if it is weak and flimsy regarding the prohibition of the subject. And shubha in the act: is that the fornicator experiences confusion regarding the prohibition of the act, i.e. the very intercourse, and it is unclear to him whether it is prohibited, without there being confusion as to the permissibility of ownership; rather the prohibition of the subject is certain due to the absence of evidence causing doubt in its permissibility. Hence, one who assumes its permissibility or claims assumption thereof is not punished with hadd, while others are punished, because this doubt is confined to those who experience it, and he is punished if...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the legal maxim "al-shubha dāri'a li-l-hadd" – doubt is a preventive of the hadd punishment. It begins by defining the key terms: shubha is that which resembles the truth but is not actually true; dāri'a means repelling. The foundational evidence is the hadith "Avert the hudud by doubts" reported through various chains, emphasizing that judges should avoid applying fixed punishments when any doubt exists. The commentator then notes that there are many reports supporting this principle, and he refers to his own treatise on the topic. He then explains that shubha is of two types: (1) shubha in the act (fi al-fi'l) – when the offender is confused about whether the act itself is prohibited, though the subject's prohibition is certain; (2) shubha in the subject (fi al-mahal) – when there is evidence suggesting the subject (e.g., the woman) might be permissible, even if weak. He also mentions a third type, shubhat al-'aqd (doubt in the contract), specific to Abu Hanifa, but notes that it is not universally accepted among the Hanafi imams; its placement is disputed. The commentary follows the author's order precisely, first defining the maxim, then providing the hadith proof, then classifying shubha, and finally explaining each type with examples from the chapter on hadd for fornication.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): What resembles the established truth but is not actually true; a doubt that prevents the application of hadd.
دارئة (dāri'a): Active participle from dar'a (to repel); a preventive or averter of the hadd punishment.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for certain crimes (e.g., fornication, theft).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of shubha are explicitly mentioned in the base matn (the original text being commented upon)?
A) One type: shubha in the act
B) Two types: shubha in the act and shubha in the subject
C) Three types: including shubhat al-'aqd
D) Four types: including shubha in the person and place

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim instructs judges to avert hadd punishments at any legally recognized doubt, classified as doubt in the act or the subject, with a third type (shubhat al-'aqd) being contested within the school.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
15 min
Two Types of Shubhah that Waive Hadd Punishment
Defines shubhah and explains the first type (shubhah fil-fi'l) with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shubhah fil-fi'l and shubhah fil-mahall and apply the hadith proof to determine when hadd is waived.

Core Idea
Shubhah drops hadd: doubt in the act or the woman excuses, but only if based on plausible assumption or proof.

In this lesson, we explore the crucial concept of shubhah (doubt) in Hanafi criminal law. The Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded us to avert hudud punishments whenever possible. This command gives rise to a detailed framework for when a person's mistaken belief can save them from the severe fixed penalties. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines two primary types of shubhah, explains their proofs, and notes a key difference of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the opening statement: 'Know that shubhah is of two types: in the act and in the subject.' Memorize these categories.
These are the agreed-upon types within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the hadith proof
Find the hadith 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can...' This is the primary dalil for leniency. Note that it is ahad (single-transmitter), so it provides zanni thubut but qati dalala.
The command 'adra'u' indicates a strong recommendation (not obligation).
3
Analyze shubhah fil-mahall (objective doubt)
This type exists when there is a legal proof that casts doubt on the permissibility of the woman herself. It does not depend on the offender's belief.
Also called shubhah hukmiyyah or shubhah fil-milk.
4
Analyze shubhah fil-fi'l (subjective doubt)
This occurs when the offender mistakenly believes the act is permissible due to a false assumption. The woman is definitively prohibited, but his assumption (claimed or genuine) creates a doubt.
This shubhah is personal: it only excuses the one who holds the assumption.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on shubhah al-'aqd
A third type (shubhah al-'aqd) is accepted only by Abu Hanifa, involving an invalid marriage contract (e.g., with a mahram). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reject it.
The author omits it here because it is not agreed upon, implying the majority view.

Source Text

اعلم أن الشبهة ضربان :
١. في الفعل.

وفي رواية ابن أبي شَيْبَة والتِّرْمِذِي والحاكم والبيهقي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها مرفوعاً: «ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم ، فإن وجدتم للمسلم مخرجاً فخلوا سبيله، فإنّ الإمام لأن يخطئ في العفو خير له من أن يخطئ في العقوبة). وفي الباب آثار كثيرة بسطتها في رسالتي : القول الجازم بسقوط الحد بنكاح المحارم»، وهي رسالة فريدة في بابها ، مَن شاءَ الاطّلاع على نفائس شريفة فليطالعها.

ويحتمل أن يكون عدم ذكرها لإدراجها في أحد القسمين، فإنّ منهم من أدرج شبهة العقد في شبهة الفعل، ومنهم من أدرجها في شبهة المحلّ.
قوله : في الفعل وفي المحل ؛ الشبهة في المحلّ؛ أي محل الوطء، وهو الموطوءة ، وتسمّى شبهة حكميّة، وشبهة في الملك أيضاً، هي أن يقوم هناك دليل ناف للحرمة في المحل في نفس الأمر من غير توقف على ظن الجاني واعتقاده، فيورث ذلك اشتباهاً ، ولو كان خفيفاً ركيكاً في حرمة المحل. والشبهة في الفعل : هي أن يكون وقعَ للواطئ اشتباه في حرمة الفعل ؛ أي نفس الوطء، واشتبه عليه كونه محرَّماً من دون أن يكون اشتباه حلّ الملك، بل حرمة المحل تكون قطعية ؛ لعدم وجود دليل يورث شبهة في حلّه ؛ ولهذا لا يحد فيه من ظنّ حلّه أو ادعى ظنّه به ، ويحد به غيره ؛ لأنّ هذه الشبهة تقتصر على من وجدت به، ويحدّ إن علم أنها حرام، بخلاف الشبهةِ الأولى.
وهي في الفعل : تثبت بظن غيرِ الدليل دليلاً، فلا يحد الجاني إن ظن أنها تحل له في وطء أمة أحد أبويه، وعرسه، وسيده، والمرتهن المرهونة
. قوله : بظـن غير الدليل ؛ أي ظنّ ما ليس دليلاً على الحل دليلاً، فهذه الشبهة تتحقق في حق من اشتبه عليه الحل والحرمة ؛ إذ لا دليل في السمع يفيد الحلّ، بل ظنّ غير الدليل دليلاً، كما يُظن أن جارية زوجته تحلّ له، لظنه أنه استخدام، واستخدامها يُظَنُّ حلال، فلا بُدَّ من الظنّ، وإلا فلا شبهة أصلاً ، لفرض أن لا دليل أصلاً لتثبت الشبهة في نفس الأمر، فلو لم يكن ظنه الحلّ ثابتاً لم تكن شبهة أصلاً. كذا في «الفتح». (٢) قوله : إن ظن ؛ أشار به إلى أن نفى الحدّ هاهنا مشروط بظن الحل، فإنّ هذا الظن هـو الشبهة ، فلو لم يظنّ لم توجد شبهة أصلاً ، بخلاف شبهة المحل، فإنّ الشبهة هناك جاءت من دليل دال على حلّ المحلّ، فلا يحتاج هناك إلى ظنّ الحلّ، ثمّ المعتبر في هذا الباب هو دعوى الظن، وإن لم يحصل له الظنّ في الواقع، ولو ادّعاه أحدهما فقط لم يحدا حتى يقراً جميعاً بعلمهما بالحرمة. كذا في «النهر». (۳) قوله : وطء أمة أحد أبويه ؛ وإن علو، كوطء أمة أم الأم وأمة الجد إلى غير ذلك، إذا ظن أنها تحل له. [٤] قوله : وعرسه ؛ - بالكسر - ؛ أي زوجته، وهو معطوف على قوله : أحد أبويه، وكذا قوله : وسيده ؛ أي أمة زوجته، وأمه سيّده. [0] قوله : والمرتهن ؛ هذا معطوف على قوله : «أمة» ؛ أي وطء المرتهن الأمة المرهونة ، ، ولا يخفى ما في هذا العطف من الاختلال، فإنّ المعطوف عليه مفعول للمضاف ؛ أي وطء، والمعطوف فاعل له، ومفعوله المرهونة، ولو قال: ومرهونة لكان أولى.
Know that shubhah is of two types: 1. In the act. 2. In the subject. And in the narration of Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Tirmidhi, al-Hakim, and al-Bayhaqi from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) as marfu': 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can, and if you find a way out for a Muslim, let him go, for the imam erring in pardon is better than erring in punishment.' And on this chapter there are many reports which I have expanded in my treatise 'The Decisive Statement on the Dropping of Hadd by Marrying Mahram Relatives', and it is a unique treatise in its field; whoever wishes to see noble treasures should consult it. He said: 'Two types' meaning two divisions, and there is a third type called shubhah al-'aqd according to Abu Hanifa, but he did not mention it because it is not agreed upon; for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad did not consider marriage as a shubhah that drops the hadd, as will come in detail if Allah wills. And it is possible that not mentioning it is because it is included in one of the two types, for some include shubhah al-'aqd in shubhah al-fi'l, and some include it in shubhah al-mahall. He said: 'In the act and in the subject': shubhah al-mahall means the place of intercourse, i.e., the woman being penetrated, and it is also called shubhah hukmiyyah, and shubhah in ownership as well; it is that there exists a proof negating prohibition in the subject in itself without depending on the offender's suspicion and belief, and this yields confusion even if it is weak and flimsy regarding the prohibition of the subject. And shubhah al-fi'l is that the fornicator experiences confusion regarding the prohibition of the act, i.e., the act of intercourse itself, and it becomes uncertain to him whether it is forbidden without there being uncertainty about the permissibility of ownership; rather the prohibition of the subject is definitive due to the absence of any proof that creates confusion about its permissibility; therefore, in this case, one who thinks it permissible or claims to think so is not subject to hadd, while another is subject to hadd; because this shubhah is limited to the person in whom it occurs, and is subject to hadd if he knows it is forbidden, unlike the first type. And it (shubhah al-fi'l) is established by assuming as proof what is not proof, so the offender is not subject to hadd if he thinks it permissible for him in the case of intercourse with the slave-girl of one of his parents, his wife, his master, and the mortgagee with a mortgaged slave-girl. He said: 'By assuming as proof what is not proof' i.e., assuming what is not proof for permissibility as proof; this shubhah occurs for one who is confused about permissibility and prohibition, for there is no transmitted proof indicating permissibility, but he assumes what is not proof as proof, such as thinking that his wife's slave-girl is permissible for him thinking it is service, and her service is thought to be halal, so assumption is necessary, otherwise there is no shubhah at all, because it is assumed that there is no proof at all to establish shubhah in itself. So if his assumption of permissibility were not established, there would be no shubhah at all. As in al-Fath. He said: 'If he thinks': indicating that the negation of hadd here is conditional upon assumption of permissibility, for this assumption is the shubhah; if he does not assume, there is no shubhah at all, unlike shubhah al-mahall, for the shubhah there comes from proof indicating the permissibility of the subject, so it does not need assumption of permissibility; then what is considered in this chapter is the claim of assumption, even if he does not actually have the assumption; if only one of them claims it, they are both subject to hadd until they both admit knowledge of prohibition. As in al-Nahr. He said: 'Intercourse with the slave-girl of one of his parents' even if paternal or maternal, like intercourse with the slave-girl of his maternal grandmother or grandfather, etc., if he thinks it is permissible for him. He said: 'and his wife' (with kasra) i.e., his spouse, and it is coordinated to 'one of his parents', and likewise his saying 'and his master' i.e., the slave-girl of his wife, and the mother of his master. He said: 'and the mortgagee' is coordinated to his saying 'slave-girl' i.e., the mortgagee's intercourse with the mortgaged slave-girl; and the defect in this coordination is not hidden, for the coordinated element is the object of the genitive i.e., 'intercourse', and the coordinator is the subject of it, and its object is 'mortgaged'; if he had said 'and a mortgaged one' it would have been better.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling regarding types of shubhah that drop the hadd punishment for zina. The author begins by stating that shubhah is of two types: shubhah fil-fi'l (in the act) and shubhah fil-mahall (in the subject). He cites a hadith from Aisha (ra) that commands averting hudud as much as possible, which is the primary proof for leniency in hadd cases. The author notes that there is a third type (shubhah al-'aqd) according to Abu Hanifa, but it is not mentioned here because it is not agreed upon by the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Then he explains each type. Shubhah fil-mahall is when there is some legal proof that casts doubt on the prohibition of the woman herself, regardless of the offender's personal belief. Shubhah fil-fi'l is when the offender mistakenly believes the act is permissible due to a false assumption, while the woman is definitively prohibited. In this second type, the hadd is waived only if the offender genuinely thought it was halal; if he knew it was haram, the hadd applies. Examples given include intercourse with a parent's slave-girl, one's wife, master's slave-girl, or a mortgaged slave-girl by the mortgagee, if he thought it was permissible. The commentary clarifies that the assumption must be claimed, even if not actually held, and if only one of multiple offenders claims it, both are subject to hadd unless both admit knowledge of prohibition.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A doubt or semblance that causes uncertainty about the prohibition of an act or subject, thereby waiving the hadd punishment.
شبهة في الفعل (shubhah fil-fi'l): Doubt arising from the offender's mistaken belief that the act itself is permissible, based on assuming a false proof.
شبهة في المحل (shubhah fil-mahall): Doubt arising from a legal proof that suggests the subject (the woman) may be permissible, regardless of the offender's belief.
شبهة العقد (shubhah al-'aqd): Doubt arising from a marriage contract that is invalid but resembles a valid contract, such as marrying a mahram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for shubhah fil-fi'l to waive the hadd punishment?
A) The presence of a marriage contract
B) The offender's genuine assumption that the act is halal
C) That the woman is a mahram
D) That both the man and woman claim ignorance

Key Takeaway
Shubhah fil-fi'l requires a personal assumption of permissibility; shubhah fil-mahall requires a objective legal proof; both are rooted in the prophetic command to avert hudud.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

Shubha fi al-Fi‘l: When Doubt Removes Hadd
Explaining shubha in action and its effect on hadd, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for shubha fi al-fi‘l to drop hadd and apply it to scenarios involving slave-girls and pawned property.

Core Idea
Hadd for zina is dropped when the offender genuinely believed the act was halal based on plausible reasoning.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a specific type of legal doubt called shubha fi al-fi‘l. Unlike shubha in the object, which arises from ambiguous legal evidence, shubha in action arises from the offender's personal belief (zann) that the act is lawful. This lesson unpacks the condition of zann, the examples given, and the rationales drawn from property connections and the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Define shubha fi al-fi‘l and distinguish from shubha fi al-mahall
Understand that shubha fi al-fi‘l is based on the offender's personal mistaken belief, while shubha fi al-mahall comes from ambiguous evidence in the object itself.
The text explicitly contrasts these two types.
2
Identify the essential condition: actual zann of halal
The offender must have genuinely believed the act was permissible. Mere ignorance or a baseless claim is insufficient.
The text says 'if he thought it (the woman) is permissible for him.'
3
List the four given examples
The text mentions: slave-girl of one's parent, wife's slave-girl, master's slave-girl, and pawned slave-girl (with a dispute).
Additional examples from the commentary: idda woman, divorce for payment, freeing umm walad.
4
Examine the two rationales
First, the connection of ownership between ancestors and descendants creates plausible doubt. Second, the husband's enrichment from wife's wealth (Quran 93:8) suggests partial ownership.
These rationales explain why the doubt is considered valid.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the pawned slave-girl
The most sound view drops hadd; a weaker view upholds it. The text sides with the asahh.
The tarjih is clear: mufta bihi is the asahh.

Book of Hudud. The coition that necessitates the hadd. It (the shubha) in action is established by deeming a non-evidence as evidence. So the offender is not subjected to hadd if he thought it (the woman) is permissible for him in intercourse with the slave-girl of one of his parents, his wife, his master, and the pawned slave-girl (according to the most sound view). The idda woman with three (talaqs), and divorce for payment, and freeing the umm walad. Know that the connection of ownership between ancestors and descendants may suggest that the son has authority to have intercourse with the father's slave-girl, as in the reverse. And the richness of the husband from the wife's wealth, derived from His saying 'And He found you poor and made you rich' meaning by the wealth of Khadija (may Allah be pleased with her), may give rise to a doubt that the wife's wealth is the husband's property.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya' expounds the rules of shubha (legal doubt) that prevents the hadd punishment for unlawful intercourse. The author first distinguishes between shubha in action (shubha fi al-fi‘l) and shubha in the object (shubha fi al-mahall). For shubha in action, the doubt arises from the perpetrator's personal belief based on something that is not a valid legal evidence. The key condition is that the offender actually believed (zanna) the act was halal; mere ignorance or careless assumption is not enough. The text lists examples: slave-girl of a parent (including ascending relatives), wife's slave-girl, master's slave-girl, and a pawned slave-girl (with a dispute over the last). The commentary adds further examples: a woman in idda of three divorces, divorce for payment, and freeing an umm walad. The rationale for exempting from hadd in these cases is twofold: (1) The connection of ownership between ancestors and descendants creates a plausible doubt that a son might think he has authority over his father's property, as the father does over the son's. (2) The husband's access to the wife's wealth is evidenced by the Quranic verse 'And He found you poor and made you rich' (al-Duha 93:8), which is interpreted as referring to the wealth of Khadija (RA) that benefited the Prophet (SAW). This suggests that the wife's property may be considered attached to the husband, generating a doubt in the case of her slave-girl. The passage also clarifies that if someone commits adultery with a married woman of his father or grandfather, the hadd is applied even if he claims he thought it lawful, because such a claim has no basis even in weak evidence. The text notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the pawned slave-girl: the most sound (asahh) view is that hadd is dropped, but there is a difference of opinion.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishment. Here specifically shubha fi al-fi‘l (doubt in action) based on a personal mistaken belief.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed for certain crimes like zina (unlawful intercourse).
ظن (zann): A belief or opinion that something is true, here specifically that the act is halal. It is the basis of shubha fi al-fi‘l.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition must be met for shubha fi al-fi‘l to drop the hadd for zina?
A) The person must be ignorant of the law entirely.
B) The person must have a genuine belief (zann) that the act is halal, based on some reasoning or perceived evidence.
C) The person must have committed the act in a state of coercion.
D) The person must be a minor or insane.

Key Takeaway
Hadd for zina is dropped when the offender had a genuine belief (zann) that the act was halal, based on plausible reasoning such as property connections or Quranic indications.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
10 min
Two Types of Shubha in Hadd for Zina: Shubhat al-Fi'l and Shubhat al-Mahall
Explains two types of shubha exempting from hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shubhat al-fi'l and shubhat al-mahall, and apply each category to determine whether hadd is applied.

Core Idea
Hadd for zina is dropped when shubha exists, but the type of shubha—act or subject—determines the legal basis.

The Hanafi school carefully delineates when the severe punishment of hadd for zina is not applied due to legal ambiguity (shubha). This lesson examines the two categories of shubha—shubhat al-fi'l and shubhat al-mahall—as explained in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. Through examples involving pledged slave-girls, women in 'iddah, and triple divorce, we will see how the presence or absence of evidence about the woman's permissibility determines which category applies and whether hadd is enforced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act in question is sexual intercourse that would normally constitute zina. The ruling is that hadd is not applied when a valid shubha (legal ambiguity) exists.
The general principle: 'Hadd is avoided by shubuhat.'
2
Define shubhat al-fi'l and its examples
Shubhat al-fi'l refers to ambiguity concerning the act itself. The person mistakenly thinks the act is permissible due to some external reason, even though the woman is unlawful. Examples include the borrower of a pledged slave-girl and a man having intercourse with a woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce.
In shubhat al-fi'l, there is no evidence indicating the woman's permissibility.
3
Define shubhat al-mahall and its examples
Shubhat al-mahall refers to ambiguity concerning the subject (the woman). There exists some legal evidence—even if weak—that the woman is permissible for the man. Examples include a woman in 'iddah after khul' or a woman who is Umm al-Walad.
This is also called shubha hukmiyya because it is based on a legal ruling.
4
Analyze the triple divorce dispute
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to the indication of Kitab al-Salah, hadd is not applied; according to Kitab al-Hudud, it is applied. The reconciliation is that the first applies when three divorces are pronounced in one word (creating shubha due to the hadith), the second when pronounced separately.
The hadith from Sahih Muslim that triple divorce was considered one in early Islam is the basis for shubha.
5
Distinguish the two types and apply
The key distinction: in shubhat al-mahall, there is direct legal evidence suggesting permissibility of the woman; in shubhat al-fi'l, there is no such evidence—only the man's mistaken assumption. Use this test to classify new scenarios.
Even if the man knows the prohibition, hadd is dropped in shubhat al-mahall due to the evidence.
